</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter09</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER IX.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>"Can I believe what you tell me? It is like a tale for men that



<P>listen only half awake by the camp fire, and it seems to have run



<P>off a woman's tongue."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Who is there here for me to deceive, O Rajah?" answered



<P>Babalatchi.&nbsp; "Without you I am nothing.&nbsp; All I have told you

I



<P>believe to be true.&nbsp; I have been safe for many years in the



<P>hollow of your hand.&nbsp; This is no time to harbour suspicions.&nbsp;

The



<P>danger is very great.&nbsp; We should advise and act at once, before



<P>the sun sets."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Right.&nbsp; Right," muttered Lakamba, pensively.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They had been sitting for the last hour together in the audience



<P>chamber of the Rajah's house, for Babalatchi, as soon as he had



<P>witnessed the landing of the Dutch officers, had crossed the



<P>river to report to his master the events of the morning, and to



<P>confer with him upon the line of conduct to pursue in the face of



<P>altered circumstances.&nbsp; They were both puzzled and frightened by



<P>the unexpected turn the events had taken.&nbsp; The Rajah, sitting



<P>crosslegged on his chair, looked fixedly at the floor; Babalatchi



<P>was squatting close by in an attitude of deep dejection.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And where did you say he is hiding now?" asked Lakamba, breaking



<P>at last the silence full of gloomy forebodings in which they both



<P>had been lost for a long while.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"In Bulangi's clearing--the furthest one, away from the house.



<P>They went there that very night.&nbsp; The white man's daughter took



<P>him there.&nbsp; She told me so herself, speaking to me openly, for



<P>she is half white and has no decency.&nbsp; She said she was waiting



<P>for him while he was here; then, after a long time, he came out



<P>of the darkness and fell at her feet exhausted.&nbsp; He lay like one



<P>dead, but she brought him back to life in her arms, and made him



<P>breathe again with her own breath.&nbsp; That is what she said,



<P>speaking to my face, as I am speaking now to you, Rajah.&nbsp; She is



<P>like a white woman and knows no shame."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He paused, deeply shocked.&nbsp; Lakamba nodded his head.&nbsp; "Well,

and



<P>then?" he asked.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"They called the old woman," went on Babalatchi, "and he told



<P>them all--about the brig, and how he tried to kill many men.&nbsp; He



<P>knew the Orang Blanda were very near, although he had said



<P>nothing to us about that; he knew his great danger.&nbsp; He thought



<P>he had killed many, but there were only two dead, as I have heard



<P>from the men of the sea that came in the warship's boats."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And the other man, he that was found in the river?" interrupted



<P>Lakamba.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"That was one of his boatmen.&nbsp; When his canoe was overturned by



<P>the logs those two swam together, but the other man must have



<P>been hurt.&nbsp; Dain swam, holding him up.&nbsp; He left him in the

bushes



<P>when he went up to the house.&nbsp; When they all came down his heart



<P>had ceased to beat; then the old woman spoke; Dain thought it was



<P>good.&nbsp; He took off his anklet and broke it, twisting it round the



<P>man's foot.&nbsp; His ring he put on that slave's hand.&nbsp; He took

off



<P>his sarong and clothed that thing that wanted no clothes, the two



<P>women holding it up meanwhile, their intent being to deceive all



<P>eyes and to mislead the minds in the settlement, so that they



<P>could swear to the thing that was not, and that there could be no



<P>treachery when the white-men came.&nbsp; Then Dain and the white woman



<P>departed to call up Bulangi and find a hiding-place.&nbsp; The old



<P>woman remained by the body."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Hai!" exclaimed Lakamba.&nbsp; "She has wisdom."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, she has a Devil of her own to whisper counsel in her ear,"



<P>assented Babalatchi.&nbsp; "She dragged the body with great toil to



<P>the point where many logs were stranded.&nbsp; All these things were



<P>done in the darkness after the storm had passed away.&nbsp; Then she



<P>waited.&nbsp; At the first sign of daylight she battered the face of



<P>the dead with a heavy stone, and she pushed him amongst the logs.



<P>She remained near, watching.&nbsp; At sunrise Mahmat Banjer came and



<P>found him.&nbsp; They all believed; I myself was deceived, but not for



<P>long.&nbsp; The white man believed, and, grieving, fled to his house.



<P>When we were alone I, having doubts, spoke to the woman, and she,



<P>fearing my anger and your might, told me all, asking for help in



<P>saving Dain."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"He must not fall into the hands of the Orang Blanda," said



<P>Lakamba; "but let him die, if the thing can be done quietly."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It cannot, Tuan!&nbsp; Remember there is that woman who, being half



<P>white, is ungovernable, and would raise a great outcry.&nbsp; Also the



<P>officers are here.&nbsp; They are angry enough already.&nbsp; Dain must



<P>escape; he must go.&nbsp; We must help him now for our own safety."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Are the officers very angry?" inquired Lakamba, with interest.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"They are.&nbsp; The principal chief used strong words when speaking



<P>to me--to me when I salaamed in your name.&nbsp; I do not think,"



<P>added Babalatchi, after a short pause and looking very



<P>worried--"I do not think I saw a white chief so angry before.&nbsp;

He



<P>said we were careless or even worse.&nbsp; He told me he would speak



<P>to the Rajah, and that I was of no account."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Speak to the Rajah!" repeated Lakamba, thoughtfully.&nbsp; "Listen,



<P>Babalatchi: I am sick, and shall withdraw; you cross over and



<P>tell the white men."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes," said Babalatchi, "I am going over at once; and as to



<P>Dain?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You get him away as you can best.&nbsp; This is a great trouble in

my



<P>heart," sighed Lakamba.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi got up, and, going close to his master, spoke



<P>earnestly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"There is one of our praus at the southern mouth of the river.



<P>The Dutch warship is to the northward watching the main entrance.



<P>I shall send Dain off to-night in a canoe, by the hidden



<P>channels, on board the prau.&nbsp; His father is a great prince, and



<P>shall hear of our generosity.&nbsp; Let the prau take him to Ampanam.



<P>Your glory shall be great, and your reward in powerful



<P>friendship.&nbsp; Almayer will no doubt deliver the dead body as



<P>Dain's to the officers, and the foolish white men shall say,



<P>'This is very good; let there be peace.' And the trouble shall be



<P>removed from your heart, Rajah."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"True! true!" said Lakamba.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And, this being accomplished by me who am your slave, you shall



<P>reward with a generous hand.&nbsp; That I know!&nbsp; The white man

is



<P>grieving for the lost treasure, in the manner of white men who



<P>thirst after dollars.&nbsp; Now, when all other things are in order,



<P>we shall perhaps obtain the treasure from the white man.&nbsp; Dain



<P>must escape, and Almayer must live."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Now go, Babalatchi, go!" said Lakamba, getting off his chair.



<P>"I am very sick, and want medicine.&nbsp; Tell the white chief so."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But Babalatchi was not to be got rid of in this summary manner.



<P>He knew that his master, after the manner of the great, liked to



<P>shift the burden of toil and danger on to his servants'



<P>shoulders, but in the difficult straits in which they were now



<P>the Rajah must play his part.&nbsp; He may be very sick for the white



<P>men, for all the world if he liked, as long as he would take upon



<P>himself the execution of part at least of Babalatchi's carefully



<P>thought-of plan.&nbsp; Babalatchi wanted a big canoe manned by twelve



<P>men to be sent out after dark towards Bulangi's clearing.&nbsp; Dain



<P>may have to be overpowered.&nbsp; A man in love cannot be expected to



<P>see clearly the path of safety if it leads him away from the



<P>object of his affections, argued Babalatchi, and in that case



<P>they would have to use force in order to make him go.&nbsp; Would the



<P>Rajah see that trusty men manned the canoe?&nbsp; The thing must be



<P>done secretly.&nbsp; Perhaps the Rajah would come himself, so as to



<P>bring all the weight of his authority to bear upon Dain if he



<P>should prove obstinate and refuse to leave his hiding-place.&nbsp; The



<P>Rajah would not commit himself to a definite promise, and



<P>anxiously pressed Babalatchi to go, being afraid of the white men



<P>paying him an unexpected visit.&nbsp; The aged statesman reluctantly



<P>took his leave and went into the courtyard.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Before going down to his boat Babalatchi stopped for a while in



<P>the big open space where the thick-leaved trees put black patches



<P>of shadow which seemed to float on a flood of smooth, intense



<P>light that rolled up to the houses and down to the stockade and



<P>over the river, where it broke and sparkled in thousands of



<P>glittering wavelets, like a band woven of azure and gold edged



<P>with the brilliant green of the forests guarding both banks of



<P>the Pantai.&nbsp; In the perfect calm before the coming of the



<P>afternoon breeze the irregularly jagged line of tree-tops stood



<P>unchanging, as if traced by an unsteady hand on the clear blue of



<P>the hot sky.&nbsp; In the space sheltered by the high palisades there



<P>lingered the smell of decaying blossoms from the surrounding



<P>forest, a taint of drying fish; with now and then a whiff of



<P>acrid smoke from the cooking fires when it eddied down from under



<P>the leafy boughs and clung lazily about the burnt-up grass.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As Babalatchi looked up at the flagstaff over-topping a group of



<P>low trees in the middle of the courtyard, the tricolour flag of



<P>the Netherlands stirred slightly for the first time since it had



<P>been hoisted that morning on the arrival of the man-of-war boats.



<P>With a faint rustle of trees the breeze came down in light puffs,



<P>playing capriciously for a time with this emblem of Lakamba's



<P>power, that was also the mark of his servitude; then the breeze



<P>freshened in a sharp gust of wind, and the flag flew out straight



<P>and steady above the trees.&nbsp; A dark shadow ran along the river,



<P>rolling over and covering up the sparkle of declining sunlight.



<P>A big white cloud sailed slowly across the darkening sky, and



<P>hung to the westward as if waiting for the sun to join it there.



<P>Men and things shook off the torpor of the hot afternoon and



<P>stirred into life under the first breath of the sea breeze.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi hurried down to the water-gate; yet before he passed



<P>through it he paused to look round the courtyard, with its light



<P>and shade, with its cheery fires, with the groups of Lakamba's



<P>soldiers and retainers scattered about.&nbsp; His own house stood



<P>amongst the other buildings in that enclosure, and the statesman



<P>of Sambir asked himself with a sinking heart when and how would



<P>it be given him to return to that house.&nbsp; He had to deal with a



<P>man more dangerous than any wild beast of his experience: a proud



<P>man, a man wilful after the manner of princes, a man in love.



<P>And he was going forth to speak to that man words of cold and



<P>worldly wisdom.&nbsp; Could anything be more appalling?&nbsp; What if

that



<P>man should take umbrage at some fancied slight to his honour or



<P>disregard of his affections and suddenly "amok"?&nbsp; The wise



<P>adviser would be the first victim, no doubt, and death would be



<P>his reward.&nbsp; And underlying the horror of this situation there



<P>was the danger of those meddlesome fools, the white men.&nbsp; A



<P>vision of comfortless exile in far-off Madura rose up before



<P>Babalatchi.&nbsp; Wouldn't that be worse than death itself?&nbsp; And

there



<P>was that half-white woman with threatening eyes.&nbsp; How could he



<P>tell what an incomprehensible creature of that sort would or



<P>would not do?&nbsp; She knew so much that she made the killing of Dain



<P>an impossibility.&nbsp; That much was certain.&nbsp; And yet the sharp,



<P>rough-edged kriss is a good and discreet friend, thought



<P>Babalatchi, as he examined his own lovingly, and put it back in



<P>the sheath, with a sigh of regret, before unfastening his canoe.



<P>As he cast off the painter, pushed out into the stream, and took



<P>up his paddle, he realised vividly how unsatisfactory it was to



<P>have women mixed up in state affairs.&nbsp; Young women, of course.



<P>For Mrs. Almayer's mature wisdom, and for the easy aptitude in



<P>intrigue that comes with years to the feminine mind, he felt the



<P>most sincere respect.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He paddled leisurely, letting the canoe drift down as he crossed



<P>towards the point.&nbsp; The sun was high yet, and nothing pressed.



<P>His work would commence only with the coming of darkness.



<P>Avoiding the Lingard jetty, he rounded the point, and paddled up



<P>the creek at the back of Almayer's house.&nbsp; There were many canoes



<P>lying there, their noses all drawn together, fastened all to the



<P>same stake.&nbsp; Babalatchi pushed his little craft in amongst them



<P>and stepped on shore.&nbsp; On the other side of the ditch something



<P>moved in the grass.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Who's that hiding?" hailed Babalatchi.&nbsp; "Come out and speak to



<P>me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Nobody answered.&nbsp; Babalatchi crossed over, passing from boat to



<P>boat, and poked his staff viciously in the suspicious place.



<P>Taminah jumped up with a cry.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What are you doing here?" he asked, surprised.&nbsp; "I have nearly



<P>stepped on your tray.&nbsp; Am I a Dyak that you should hide at my



<P>sight?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I was weary, and--I slept," whispered Taminah, confusedly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You slept! You have not sold anything to-day, and you will be



<P>beaten when you return home," said Babalatchi.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Taminah stood before him abashed and silent.&nbsp; Babalatchi looked



<P>her over carefully with great satisfaction.&nbsp; Decidedly he would



<P>offer fifty dollars more to that thief Bulangi.&nbsp; The girl pleased



<P>him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Now you go home.&nbsp; It is late," he said sharply.&nbsp; "Tell Bulangi



<P>that I shall be near his house before the night is half over, and



<P>that I want him to make all things ready for a long journey.&nbsp; You



<P>understand?&nbsp; A long journey to the southward.&nbsp; Tell him that



<P>before sunset, and do not forget my words."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Taminah made a gesture of assent, and watched Babalatchi recross



<P>the ditch and disappear through the bushes bordering Almayer's



<P>compound.&nbsp; She moved a little further off the creek and sank in



<P>the grass again, lying down on her face, shivering in dry-eyed



<P>misery.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi walked straight towards the cooking-shed looking for



<P>Mrs. Almayer.&nbsp; The courtyard was in a great uproar.&nbsp; A strange



<P>Chinaman had possession of the kitchen fire and was noisily



<P>demanding another saucepan.&nbsp; He hurled objurgations, in the



<P>Canton dialect and bad Malay, against the group of slave-girls



<P>standing a little way off, half frightened, half amused, at his



<P>violence.&nbsp; From the camping fires round which the seamen of the



<P>frigate were sitting came words of encouragement, mingled with



<P>laughter and jeering.&nbsp; In the midst of this noise and confusion



<P>Babalatchi met Ali, an empty dish in his hand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Where are the white men?" asked Babalatchi.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"They are eating in the front verandah," answered Ali.&nbsp; "Do not



<P>stop me, Tuan.&nbsp; I am giving the white men their food and am



<P>busy."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Where's Mem Almayer?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Inside in the passage.&nbsp; She is listening to the talk."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Ali grinned and passed on; Babalatchi ascended the plankway to



<P>the rear verandah, and beckoning out Mrs. Almayer, engaged her in



<P>earnest conversation.&nbsp; Through the long passage, closed at the



<P>further end by the red curtain, they could hear from time to time



<P>Almayer's voice mingling in conversation with an abrupt loudness



<P>that made Mrs. Almayer look significantly at Babalatchi.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Listen," she said.&nbsp; "He has drunk much."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"He has," whispered Babalatchi.&nbsp; "He will sleep heavily



<P>to-night."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs. Almayer looked doubtful.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Sometimes the devil of strong gin makes him keep awake, and he



<P>walks up and down the verandah all night, cursing; then we stand



<P>afar off," explained Mrs. Almayer, with the fuller knowledge born



<P>of twenty odd years of married life.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But then he does not hear, nor understand, and his hand, of



<P>course, has no strength.&nbsp; We do not want him to hear to-night."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No," assented Mrs. Almayer, energetically, but in a cautiously



<P>subdued voice.&nbsp; "If he hears he will kill."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi looked incredulous.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Hai Tuan, you may believe me.&nbsp; Have I not lived many years with



<P>that man?&nbsp; Have I not seen death in that man's eyes more than



<P>once when I was younger and he guessed at many things.&nbsp; Had he



<P>been a man of my own people I would not have seen such a look



<P>twice; but he--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>With a contemptuous gesture she seemed to fling unutterable scorn



<P>on Almayer's weak-minded aversion to sudden bloodshed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"If he has the wish but not the strength, then what do we fear?"



<P>asked Babalatchi, after a short silence during which they both



<P>listened to Almayer's loud talk till it subsided into the murmur



<P>of general conversation.&nbsp; "What do we fear?" repeated Babalatchi



<P>again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"To keep the daughter whom he loves he would strike into your



<P>heart and mine without hesitation," said Mrs. Almayer.&nbsp; "When the



<P>girl is gone he will be like the devil unchained.&nbsp; Then you and

I



<P>had better beware."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I am an old man and fear not death," answered Babalatchi, with a



<P>mendacious assumption of indifference.&nbsp; "But what will you do?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I am an old woman, and wish to live," retorted Mrs. Almayer.



<P>"She is my daughter also.&nbsp; I shall seek safety at the feet of our



<P>Rajah, speaking in the name of the past when we both were young,



<P>and he--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi raised his hand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Enough.&nbsp; You shall be protected," he said soothingly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Again the sound of Almayer's voice was heard, and again



<P>interrupting their talk, they listened to the confused but loud



<P>utterance coming in bursts of unequal strength, with unexpected



<P>pauses and noisy repetitions that made some words and sentences



<P>fall clear and distinct on their ears out of the meaningless



<P>jumble of excited shoutings emphasised by the thumping of



<P>Almayer's fist upon the table.&nbsp; On the short intervals of



<P>silence, the high complaining note of tumblers, standing close



<P>together and vibrating to the shock, lingered, growing fainter,



<P>till it leapt up again into tumultuous ringing, when a new idea



<P>started a new rush of words and brought down the heavy hand



<P>again.&nbsp; At last the quarrelsome shouting ceased, and the thin



<P>plaint of disturbed glass died away into reluctant quietude.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi and Mrs. Almayer had listened curiously, their bodies



<P>bent and their ears turned towards the passage.&nbsp; At every louder



<P>shout they nodded at each other with a ridiculous affectation of



<P>scandalised propriety, and they remained in the same attitude for



<P>some time after the noise had ceased.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"This is the devil of gin," whispered Mrs. Almayer.&nbsp; "Yes; he



<P>talks like that sometimes when there is nobody to hear him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What does he say?" inquired Babalatchi, eagerly.&nbsp; "You ought to



<P>understand."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I have forgotten their talk.&nbsp; A little I understood.&nbsp; He

spoke



<P>without any respect of the white ruler in Batavia, and of



<P>protection, and said he had been wronged; he said that several



<P>times.&nbsp; More I did not understand.&nbsp; Listen!&nbsp; Again he

speaks!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Tse! tse! tse!" clicked Babalatchi, trying to appear shocked,



<P>but with a joyous twinkle of his solitary eye.&nbsp; "There will be



<P>great trouble between those white men.&nbsp; I will go round now and



<P>see.&nbsp; You tell your daughter that there is a sudden and a long



<P>journey before her, with much glory and splendour at the end.



<P>And tell her that Dain must go, or he must die, and that he will



<P>not go alone."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No, he will not go alone," slowly repeated Mrs. Almayer, with a



<P>thoughtful air, as she crept into the passage after seeing



<P>Babalatchi disappear round the corner of the house.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The statesman of Sambir, under the impulse of vivid curiosity,



<P>made his way quickly to the front of the house, but once there he



<P>moved slowly and cautiously as he crept step by step up the



<P>stairs of the verandah.&nbsp; On the highest step he sat down quietly,



<P>his feet on the steps below, ready for flight should his presence



<P>prove unwelcome.&nbsp; He felt pretty safe so.&nbsp; The table stood

nearly



<P>endways to him, and he saw Almayer's back; at Nina he looked full



<P>face, and had a side view of both officers; but of the four



<P>persons sitting at the table only Nina and the younger officer



<P>noticed his noiseless arrival.&nbsp; The momentary dropping of Nina's



<P>eyelids acknowledged Babalatchi's presence; she then spoke at



<P>once to the young sub, who turned towards her with attentive



<P>alacrity, but her gaze was fastened steadily on her father's face



<P>while Almayer was speaking uproariously.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>" . . . disloyalty and unscrupulousness!&nbsp; What have you ever done



<P>to make me loyal?&nbsp; You have no grip on this country.&nbsp; I had

to



<P>take care of myself, and when I asked for protection I was met



<P>with threats and contempt, and had Arab slander thrown in my



<P>face.&nbsp; I! a white man!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Don't be violent, Almayer," remonstrated the lieutenant; "I have



<P>heard all this already."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Then why do you talk to me about scruples?&nbsp; I wanted money, and



<P>I gave powder in exchange.&nbsp; How could I know that some of your



<P>wretched men were going to be blown up?&nbsp; Scruples!&nbsp; Pah!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He groped unsteadily amongst the bottles, trying one after



<P>another, grumbling to himself the while.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No more wine," he muttered discontentedly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You have had enough, Almayer," said the lieutenant, as he



<P>lighted a cigar.&nbsp; "Is it not time to deliver to us your prisoner?



<P>I take it you have that Dain Maroola stowed away safely



<P>somewhere.&nbsp; Still we had better get that business over, and then



<P>we shall have more drink.&nbsp; Come! don't look at me like this."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer was staring with stony eyes, his trembling fingers



<P>fumbling about his throat.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Gold," he said with difficulty.&nbsp; "Hem!&nbsp; A hand on the windpipe,



<P>you know.&nbsp; Sure you will excuse.&nbsp; I wanted to say--a little

gold



<P>for a little powder.&nbsp; What's that?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I know, I know," said the lieutenant soothingly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No! You don't know.&nbsp; Not one of you knows!" shouted Almayer.



<P>"The government is a fool, I tell you.&nbsp; Heaps of gold.&nbsp; I

am the



<P>man that knows; I and another one.&nbsp; But he won't speak.&nbsp; He

is--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He checked himself with a feeble smile, and, making an



<P>unsuccessful attempt to pat the officer on the shoulder, knocked



<P>over a couple of empty bottles.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Personally you are a fine fellow," he said very distinctly, in a



<P>patronising manner.&nbsp; His head nodded drowsily as he sat muttering



<P>to himself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The two officers looked at each other helplessly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"This won't do," said the lieutenant, addressing his junior.



<P>"Have the men mustered in the compound here.&nbsp; I must get some



<P>sense out of him.&nbsp; Hi!&nbsp; Almayer! Wake up, man.&nbsp; Redeem

your word.



<P>You gave your word.&nbsp; You gave your word of honour, you know."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer shook off the officer's hand with impatience, but his



<P>ill-humour vanished at once, and he looked up, putting his



<P>forefinger to the side of his nose.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You are very young; there is time for all things," he said, with



<P>an air of great sagacity.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The lieutenant turned towards Nina, who, leaning back in her



<P>chair, watched her father steadily.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Really I am very much distressed by all this for your sake," he



<P>exclaimed.&nbsp; "I do not know;" he went on, speaking with some



<P>embarrassment, "whether I have any right to ask you anything,



<P>unless, perhaps, to withdraw from this painful scene, but I feel



<P>that I must--for your father's good--suggest that you should--I



<P>mean if you have any influence over him you ought to exert it now



<P>to make him keep the promise he gave me before he--before he got



<P>into this state."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He observed with discouragement that she seemed not to take any



<P>notice of what he said sitting still with half-closed eyes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I trust--" he began again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What is the promise you speak of?" abruptly asked Nina, leaving



<P>her seat and moving towards her father.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Nothing that is not just and proper.&nbsp; He promised to deliver to



<P>us a man who in time of profound peace took the lives of innocent



<P>men to escape the punishment he deserved for breaking the law.



<P>He planned his mischief on a large scale.&nbsp; It is not his fault

if



<P>it failed, partially.&nbsp; Of course you have heard of Dain Maroola.



<P>Your father secured him, I understand.&nbsp; We know he escaped up



<P>this river.&nbsp; Perhaps you--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And he killed white men!" interrupted Nina.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I regret to say they were white.&nbsp; Yes, two white men lost their



<P>lives through that scoundrel's freak."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Two only!" exclaimed Nina.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The officer looked at her in amazement.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Why! why! You- " he stammered, confused.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"There might have been more," interrupted Nina.&nbsp; "And when you



<P>get this--this scoundrel will you go?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The lieutenant, still speechless, bowed his assent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Then I would get him for you if I had to seek him in a burning



<P>fire," she burst out with intense energy.&nbsp; "I hate the sight of



<P>your white faces.&nbsp; I hate the sound of your gentle voices.&nbsp;

That



<P>is the way you speak to women, dropping sweet words before any



<P>pretty face.&nbsp; I have heard your voices before.&nbsp; I hoped to

live



<P>here without seeing any other white face but this," she added in



<P>a gentler tone, touching lightly her father's cheek.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer ceased his mumbling and opened his eyes.&nbsp; He caught hold



<P>of his daughter's hand and pressed it to his face, while Nina



<P>with the other hand smoothed his rumpled grey hair, looking



<P>defiantly over her father's head at the officer, who had now



<P>regained his composure and returned her look with a cool, steady



<P>stare.&nbsp; Below, in front of the verandah, they could hear the



<P>tramp of seamen mustering there according to orders.&nbsp; The



<P>sub-lieutenant came up the steps, while Babalatchi stood up



<P>uneasily and, with finger on lip, tried to catch Nina's eye.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You are a good girl," whispered Almayer, absently, dropping his



<P>daughter's hand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Father! father!" she cried, bending over him with passionate



<P>entreaty.&nbsp; "See those two men looking at us.&nbsp; Send them away.&nbsp;

I



<P>cannot bear it any more.&nbsp; Send them away.&nbsp; Do what they want

and



<P>let them go."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She caught sight of Babalatchi and ceased speaking suddenly, but



<P>her foot tapped the floor with rapid beats in a paroxysm of



<P>nervous restlessness.&nbsp; The two officers stood close together



<P>looking on curiously.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What has happened?&nbsp; What is the matter?" whispered the younger



<P>man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Don't know," answered the other, under his breath.&nbsp; "One is



<P>furious, and the other is drunk.&nbsp; Not so drunk, either.&nbsp; Queer,



<P>this.&nbsp; Look!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer had risen, holding on to his daughter's arm.&nbsp; He



<P>hesitated a moment, then he let go his hold and lurched half-way



<P>across the verandah.&nbsp; There he pulled himself together, and stood



<P>very straight, breathing hard and glaring round angrily.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Are the men ready?" asked the lieutenant.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"All ready, sir."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Now, Mr. Almayer, lead the way," said the lieutenant

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer rested his eyes on him as if he saw him for the first



<P>time.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Two men," he said thickly.&nbsp; The effort of speaking seemed to



<P>interfere with his equilibrium.&nbsp; He took a quick step to save



<P>himself from a fall, and remained swaying backwards and forwards.



<P>"Two men," he began again, speaking with difficulty.&nbsp; "Two white



<P>men--men in uniform--honourable men.&nbsp; I want to say--men of



<P>honour.&nbsp; Are you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Come! None of that," said the officer impatiently.&nbsp; "Let us have



<P>that friend of yours."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What do you think I am?" asked Almayer, fiercely.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You are drunk, but not so drunk as not to know what you are



<P>doing.&nbsp; Enough of this tomfoolery," said the officer sternly, "or



<P>I will have you put under arrest in your own house."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Arrest!" laughed Almayer, discordantly.&nbsp; "Ha! ha! ha!&nbsp; Arrest!



<P>Why, I have been trying to get out of this infernal place for



<P>twenty years, and I can't.&nbsp; You hear, man! I can't, and never



<P>shall!&nbsp; Never!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He ended his words with a sob, and walked unsteadily down the



<P>stairs.&nbsp; When in the courtyard the lieutenant approached him, and



<P>took him by the arm.&nbsp; The sub-lieutenant and Babalatchi followed



<P>close.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"That's better, Almayer," said the officer encouragingly.&nbsp; "Where



<P>are you going to?&nbsp; There are only planks there.&nbsp; Here," he

went



<P>on, shaking him slightly, "do we want the boats?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No," answered Almayer, viciously.&nbsp; "You want a grave."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What?&nbsp; Wild again!&nbsp; Try to talk sense."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Grave!" roared Almayer, struggling to get himself free.&nbsp; "A hole



<P>in the ground.&nbsp; Don't you understand?&nbsp; You must be drunk.&nbsp;

Let me



<P>go!&nbsp; Let go, I tell you!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He tore away from the officer's grasp, and reeled towards the



<P>planks where the body lay under its white cover; then he turned



<P>round quickly, and faced the semicircle of interested faces.&nbsp; The



<P>sun was sinking rapidly, throwing long shadows of house and trees



<P>over the courtyard, but the light lingered yet on the river,



<P>where the logs went drifting past in midstream, looking very



<P>distinct and black in the pale red glow.&nbsp; The trunks of the trees



<P>in the forest on the east bank were lost in gloom while their



<P>highest branches swayed gently in the departing sunlight.&nbsp; The



<P>air felt heavy and cold in the breeze, expiring in slight puffs



<P>that came over the water.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer shivered as he made an effort to speak, and again with an



<P>uncertain gesture he seemed to free his throat from the grip of



<P>an invisible hand.&nbsp; His bloodshot eyes wandered aimlessly from



<P>face to face.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"There!" he said at last.&nbsp; "Are you all there?&nbsp; He is a dangerous



<P>man."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He dragged at the cover with hasty violence, and the body rolled



<P>stiffly off the planks and fell at his feet in rigid



<P>helplessness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Cold, perfectly cold," said Almayer, looking round with a



<P>mirthless smile.&nbsp; "Sorry can do no better.&nbsp; And you can't

hang



<P>him, either.&nbsp; As you observe, gentlemen," he added gravely,



<P>"there is no head, and hardly any neck."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The last ray of light was snatched away from the tree-tops, the



<P>river grew suddenly dark, and in the great stillness the murmur



<P>of the flowing water seemed to fill the vast expanse of grey



<P>shadow that descended upon the land.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"This is Dain," went on Almayer to the silent group that



<P>surrounded him.&nbsp; "And I have kept my word.&nbsp; First one hope,

then



<P>another, and this is my last.&nbsp; Nothing is left now.&nbsp; You think



<P>there is one dead man here?&nbsp; Mistake, I 'sure you.&nbsp; I am much



<P>more dead.&nbsp; Why don't you hang me?" he suggested suddenly, in a



<P>friendly tone, addressing the lieutenant.&nbsp; "I assure, assure you



<P>it would be a mat--matter of form altog--altogether."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>These last words he muttered to himself, and walked zigzaging



<P>towards his house.&nbsp; "Get out!" he thundered at Ali, who was



<P>approaching timidly with offers of assistance.&nbsp; From afar, scared



<P>groups of men and women watched his devious progress.&nbsp; He dragged



<P>himself up the stairs by the banister, and managed to reach a



<P>chair into which he fell heavily.&nbsp; He sat for awhile panting with



<P>exertion and anger, and looking round vaguely for Nina; then



<P>making a threatening gesture towards the compound, where he had



<P>heard Babalatchi's voice, he overturned the table with his foot



<P>in a great crash of smashed crockery.&nbsp; He muttered yet menacingly



<P>to himself, then his head fell on his breast, his eyes closed,



<P>and with a deep sigh he fell asleep.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>That night--for the first time in its history--the peaceful and



<P>flourishing settlement of Sambir saw the lights shining about



<P>"Almayer's Folly."&nbsp; These were the lanterns of the boats hung up



<P>by the seamen under the verandah where the two officers were



<P>holding a court of inquiry into the truth of the story related to



<P>them by Babalatchi.&nbsp; Babalatchi had regained all his importance.



<P>He was eloquent and persuasive, calling Heaven and Earth to



<P>witness the truth of his statements.&nbsp; There were also other



<P>witnesses.&nbsp; Mahmat Banjer and a good many others underwent a



<P>close examination that dragged its weary length far into the



<P>evening.&nbsp; A messenger was sent for Abdulla, who excused himself



<P>from coming on the score of his venerable age, but sent Reshid.



<P>Mahmat had to produce the bangle, and saw with rage and



<P>mortification the lieutenant put it in his pocket, as one of the



<P>proofs of Dain's death, to be sent in with the official report of



<P>the mission.&nbsp; Babalatchi's ring was also impounded for the same



<P>purpose, but the experienced statesman was resigned to that loss



<P>from the very beginning.&nbsp; He did not mind as long as he was sure,



<P>that the white men believed.&nbsp; He put that question to himself



<P>earnestly as he left, one of the last, when the proceedings came



<P>to a close.&nbsp; He was not certain.&nbsp; Still, if they believed

only



<P>for a night, he would put Dain beyond their reach and feel safe



<P>himself.&nbsp; He walked away fast, looking from time to time over his



<P>shoulder in the fear of being followed, but he saw and heard



<P>nothing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Ten o'clock," said the lieutenant, looking at his watch and



<P>yawning.&nbsp; "I shall hear some of the captain's complimentary



<P>remarks when we get back.&nbsp; Miserable business, this."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Do you think all this is true?" asked the younger man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"True! It is just possible.&nbsp; But if it isn't true what can we do?



<P>If we had a dozen boats we could patrol the creeks; and that



<P>wouldn't be much good.&nbsp; That drunken madman was right; we haven't



<P>enough hold on this coast.&nbsp; They do what they like.&nbsp; Are our



<P>hammocks slung?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, I told the coxswain.&nbsp; Strange couple over there," said the



<P>sub, with a wave of his hand towards Almayer's house.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Hem! Queer, certainly.&nbsp; What have you been telling her?&nbsp;

I was



<P>attending to the father most of the time."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I assure you I have been perfectly civil," protested the other



<P>warmly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"All right.&nbsp; Don't get excited.&nbsp; She objects to civility,

then,



<P>from what I understand.&nbsp; I thought you might have been tender.



<P>You know we are on service."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, of course.&nbsp; Never forget that.&nbsp; Coldly civil.&nbsp;

That's



<P>all."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They both laughed a little, and not feeling sleepy began to pace



<P>the verandah side by side.&nbsp; The moon rose stealthily above the



<P>trees, and suddenly changed the river into a stream of



<P>scintillating silver.&nbsp; The forest came out of the black void and



<P>stood sombre and pensive over the sparkling water.&nbsp; The breeze



<P>died away into a breathless calm.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Seamanlike, the two officers tramped measuredly up and down



<P>without exchanging a word.&nbsp; The loose planks rattled rhythmically



<P>under their steps with obstrusive dry sound in the perfect



<P>silence of the night.&nbsp; As they were wheeling round again the



<P>younger man stood attentive.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Did you hear that?" he asked.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No!" said the other.&nbsp; "Hear what?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I thought I heard a cry.&nbsp; Ever so faint.&nbsp; Seemed a woman's



<P>voice.&nbsp; In that other house.&nbsp; Ah!&nbsp; Again!&nbsp; Hear

it?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No," said the lieutenant, after listening awhile.&nbsp; "You young



<P>fellows always hear women's voices.&nbsp; If you are going to dream



<P>you had better get into your hammock.&nbsp; Good-night."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The moon mounted higher, and the warm shadows grew smaller and



<P>crept away as if hiding before the cold and cruel light.



<P>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER VII.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>The bright sunshine of the clear mistless morning, after the



<P>stormy night, flooded the main path of the settlement leading



<P>from the low shore of the Pantai branch of the river to the gate



<P>of Abdulla's compound.&nbsp; The path was deserted this morning; it



<P>stretched its dark yellow surface, hard beaten by the tramp of



<P>many bare feet, between the clusters of palm trees, whose tall



<P>trunks barred it with strong black lines at irregular intervals,



<P>while the newly risen sun threw the shadows of their leafy heads



<P>far away over the roofs of the buildings lining the river, even



<P>over the river itself as it flowed swiftly and silently past the



<P>deserted houses.&nbsp; For the houses were deserted too.&nbsp; On the



<P>narrow strip of trodden grass intervening between their open



<P>doors and the road, the morning fires smouldered untended,



<P>sending thin fluted columns of smoke into the cool air, and



<P>spreading the thinnest veil of mysterious blue haze over the



<P>sunlit solitude of the settlement.&nbsp; Almayer, just out of his



<P>hammock, gazed sleepily at the unwonted appearance of Sambir,



<P>wondering vaguely at the absence of life.&nbsp; His own house was very



<P>quiet; he could not hear his wife's voice, nor the sound of



<P>Nina's footsteps in the big room, opening on the verandah, which



<P>he called his sitting-room, whenever, in the company of white



<P>men, he wished to assert his claims to the commonplace decencies



<P>of civilisation.&nbsp; Nobody ever sat there; there was nothing there



<P>to sit upon, for Mrs. Almayer in her savage moods, when excited



<P>by the reminiscences of the piratical period of her life, had



<P>torn off the curtains to make sarongs for the slave-girls, and



<P>had burnt the showy furniture piecemeal to cook the family rice.



<P>But Almayer was not thinking of his furniture now.&nbsp; He was



<P>thinking of Dain's return, of Dain's nocturnal interview with



<P>Lakamba, of its possible influence on his long-matured plans, now



<P>nearing the period of their execution.&nbsp; He was also uneasy at the



<P>non-appearance of Dain who had promised him an early visit.&nbsp; "The



<P>fellow had plenty of time to cross the river," he mused, "and



<P>there was so much to be done to-day.&nbsp; The settling of details for



<P>the early start on the morrow; the launching of the boats; the



<P>thousand and one finishing touches.&nbsp; For the expedition must



<P>start complete, nothing should be forgotten, nothing should--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The sense of the unwonted solitude grew upon him suddenly, and in



<P>the unusual silence he caught himself longing even for the



<P>usually unwelcome sound of his wife's voice to break the



<P>oppressive stillness which seemed, to his frightened fancy, to



<P>portend the advent of some new misfortune.&nbsp; "What has happened?"



<P>he muttered half aloud, as he shuffled in his imperfectly



<P>adjusted slippers towards the balustrade of the verandah.&nbsp; "Is



<P>everybody asleep or dead?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The settlement was alive and very much awake.&nbsp; It was awake ever



<P>since the early break of day, when Mahmat Banjer, in a fit of



<P>unheard-of energy, arose and, taking up his hatchet, stepped over



<P>the sleeping forms of his two wives and walked shivering to the



<P>water's edge to make sure that the new house he was building had



<P>not floated away during the night.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The house was being built by the enterprising Mahmat on a large



<P>raft, and he had securely moored it just inside the muddy point



<P>of land at the junction of the two branches of the Pantai so as



<P>to be out of the way of drifting logs that would no doubt strand



<P>on the point during the freshet.&nbsp; Mahmat walked through the wet



<P>grass saying bourrouh, and cursing softly to himself the hard



<P>necessities of active life that drove him from his warm couch



<P>into the cold of the morning.&nbsp; A glance showed him that his house



<P>was still there, and he congratulated himself on his foresight in



<P>hauling it out of harm's way, for the increasing light showed him



<P>a confused wrack of drift-logs, half-stranded on the muddy flat,



<P>interlocked into a shapeless raft by their branches, tossing to



<P>and fro and grinding together in the eddy caused by the meeting



<P>currents of the two branches of the river.&nbsp; Mahmat walked down

to



<P>the water's edge to examine the rattan moorings of his house just



<P>as the sun cleared the trees of the forest on the opposite shore.



<P>As he bent over the fastenings he glanced again carelessly at the



<P>unquiet jumble of logs and saw there something that caused him to



<P>drop his hatchet and stand up, shading his eyes with his hand



<P>from the rays of the rising sun.&nbsp; It was something red, and the



<P>logs rolled over it, at times closing round it, sometimes hiding



<P>it.&nbsp; It looked to him at first like a strip of red cloth.&nbsp;

The



<P>next moment Mahmat had made it out and raised a great shout.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Ah ya! There!" yelled Mahmat.&nbsp; "There's a man amongst the logs."



<P>He put the palms of his hand to his lips and shouted, enunciating



<P>distinctly, his face turned towards the settlement: "There's a



<P>body of a man in the river! Come and see! A dead--stranger!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The women of the nearest house were already outside kindling the



<P>fires and husking the morning rice.&nbsp; They took up the cry



<P>shrilly, and it travelled so from house to house, dying away in



<P>the distance.&nbsp; The men rushed out excited but silent, and ran



<P>towards the muddy point where the unconscious logs tossed and



<P>ground and bumped and rolled over the dead stranger with the



<P>stupid persistency of inanimate things.&nbsp; The women followed,



<P>neglecting their domestic duties and disregarding the



<P>possibilities of domestic discontent, while groups of children



<P>brought up the rear, warbling joyously, in the delight of



<P>unexpected excitement.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer called aloud for his wife and daughter, but receiving no



<P>response, stood listening intently.&nbsp; The murmur of the crowd



<P>reached him faintly, bringing with it the assurance of some



<P>unusual event.&nbsp; He glanced at the river just as he was going to



<P>leave the verandah and checked himself at the sight of a small



<P>canoe crossing over from the Rajah's landing-place.&nbsp; The solitary



<P>occupant (in whom Almayer soon recognised Babalatchi) effected



<P>the crossing a little below the house and paddled up to the



<P>Lingard jetty in the dead water under the bank.&nbsp; Babalatchi



<P>clambered out slowly and went on fastening his canoe with



<P>fastidious care, as if not in a hurry to meet Almayer, whom he



<P>saw looking at him from the verandah.&nbsp; This delay gave Almayer



<P>time to notice and greatly wonder at Babalatchi's official



<P>get-up.&nbsp; The statesman of Sambir was clad in a costume befitting



<P>his high rank.&nbsp; A loudly checkered sarong encircled his waist,



<P>and from its many folds peeped out the silver hilt of the kriss



<P>that saw the light only on great festivals or during official



<P>receptions.&nbsp; Over the left shoulder and across the otherwise



<P>unclad breast of the aged diplomatist glistened a patent leather



<P>belt bearing a brass plate with the arms of Netherlands under the



<P>inscription, "Sultan of Sambir."&nbsp; Babalatchi's head was covered



<P>by a red turban, whose fringed ends falling over the left cheek



<P>and shoulder gave to his aged face a ludicrous expression of



<P>joyous recklessness.&nbsp; When the canoe was at last fastened to his



<P>satisfaction he straightened himself up, shaking down the folds



<P>of his sarong, and moved with long strides towards Almayer's



<P>house, swinging regularly his long ebony staff, whose gold head



<P>ornamented with precious stones flashed in the morning sun.



<P>Almayer waved his hand to the right towards the point of land, to



<P>him invisible, but in full view from the jetty.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, Babalatchi! oh!" he called out; "what is the matter there?



<P>can you see?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi stopped and gazed intently at the crowd on the river



<P>bank, and after a little while the astonished Almayer saw him



<P>leave the path, gather up his sarong in one hand, and break into



<P>a trot through the grass towards the muddy point.&nbsp; Almayer, now



<P>greatly interested, ran down the steps of the verandah.&nbsp; The



<P>murmur of men's voices and the shrill cries of women reached him



<P>quite distinctly now, and as soon as he turned the corner of his



<P>house he could see the crowd on the low promontory swaying and



<P>pushing round some object of interest.&nbsp; He could indistinctly



<P>hear Babalatchi's voice, then the crowd opened before the aged



<P>statesman and closed after him with an excited hum, ending in a



<P>loud shout.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As Almayer approached the throng a man ran out and rushed past



<P>him towards the settlement, unheeding his call to stop and



<P>explain the cause of this excitement.&nbsp; On the very outskirts of



<P>the crowd Almayer found himself arrested by an unyielding mass of



<P>humanity, regardless of his entreaties for a passage, insensible



<P>to his gentle pushes as he tried to work his way through it



<P>towards the riverside.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In the midst of his gentle and slow progress he fancied suddenly



<P>he had heard his wife's voice in the thickest of the throng.&nbsp; He



<P>could not mistake very well Mrs. Almayer's high-pitched tones,



<P>yet the words were too indistinct for him to understand their



<P>purport.&nbsp; He paused in his endeavours to make a passage for



<P>himself, intending to get some intelligence from those around



<P>him, when a long and piercing shriek rent the air, silencing the



<P>murmurs of the crowd and the voices of his informants.&nbsp; For a



<P>moment Almayer remained as if turned into stone with astonishment



<P>and horror, for he was certain now that he had heard his wife



<P>wailing for the dead.&nbsp; He remembered Nina's unusual absence, and



<P>maddened by his apprehensions as to her safety, he pushed blindly



<P>and violently forward, the crowd falling back with cries of



<P>surprise and pain before his frantic advance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>On the point of land in a little clear space lay the body of the



<P>stranger just hauled out from amongst the logs.&nbsp; On one side



<P>stood Babalatchi, his chin resting on the head of his staff and



<P>his one eye gazing steadily at the shapeless mass of broken



<P>limbs, torn flesh, and bloodstained rags.&nbsp; As Almayer burst



<P>through the ring of horrified spectators, Mrs. Almayer threw her



<P>own head-veil over the upturned face of the drowned man, and,



<P>squatting by it, with another mournful howl, sent a shiver



<P>through the now silent crowd.&nbsp; Mahmat, dripping wet, turned to



<P>Almayer, eager to tell his tale.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In the first moment of reaction from the anguish of his fear the



<P>sunshine seemed to waver before Almayer's eyes, and he listened



<P>to words spoken around him without comprehending their meaning.



<P>When, by a strong effort of will, he regained the possession of



<P>his senses, Mahmat was saying--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"That is the way, Tuan.&nbsp; His sarong was caught in the broken



<P>branch, and he hung with his head under water.&nbsp; When I saw what



<P>it was I did not want it here.&nbsp; I wanted it to get clear and



<P>drift away.&nbsp; Why should we bury a stranger in the midst of our



<P>houses for his ghost to frighten our women and children?&nbsp; Have

we



<P>not enough ghosts about this place?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A murmur of approval interrupted him here.&nbsp; Mahmat looked



<P>reproachfully at Babalatchi.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But the Tuan Babalatchi ordered me to drag the body ashore"--he



<P>went on looking round at his audience, but addressing himself



<P>only to Almayer--"and I dragged him by the feet; in through the



<P>mud I have dragged him, although my heart longed to see him float



<P>down the river to strand perchance on Bulangi's clearing--may his



<P>father's grave be defiled!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was subdued laughter at this, for the enmity of Mahmat and



<P>Bulangi was a matter of common notoriety and of undying interest



<P>to the inhabitants of Sambir.&nbsp; In the midst of that mirth Mrs.



<P>Almayer wailed suddenly again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Allah!&nbsp; What ails the woman!" exclaimed Mahmat, angrily.&nbsp;

"Here,



<P>I have touched this carcass which came from nobody knows where,



<P>and have most likely defiled myself before eating rice.&nbsp; By



<P>orders of Tuan Babalatchi I did this thing to please the white



<P>man.&nbsp; Are you pleased, O Tuan Almayer?&nbsp; And what will be my



<P>recompense?&nbsp; Tuan Babalatchi said a recompense there will be, and



<P>from you.&nbsp; Now consider.&nbsp; I have been defiled, and if not

defiled



<P>I may be under the spell.&nbsp; Look at his anklets!&nbsp; Who ever

heard



<P>of a corpse appearing during the night amongst the logs with gold



<P>anklets on its legs?&nbsp; There is witchcraft there.&nbsp; However,"

added



<P>Mahmat, after a reflective pause, "I will have the anklet if



<P>there is permission, for I have a charm against the ghosts and am



<P>not afraid.&nbsp; God is great!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A fresh outburst of noisy grief from Mrs. Almayer checked the



<P>flow of Mahmat's eloquence.&nbsp; Almayer, bewildered, looked in turn



<P>at his wife, at Mahmat, at Babalatchi, and at last arrested his



<P>fascinated gaze on the body lying on the mud with covered face in



<P>a grotesquely unnatural contortion of mangled and broken limbs,



<P>one twisted and lacerated arm, with white bones protruding in



<P>many places through the torn flesh, stretched out; the hand with



<P>outspread fingers nearly touching his foot.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Do you know who this is?" he asked of Babalatchi, in a low



<P>voice.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi, staring straight before him, hardly moved his lips,



<P>while Mrs. Almayer's persistent lamentations drowned the whisper



<P>of his murmured reply intended only for Almayer's ear.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It was fate.&nbsp; Look at your feet, white man.&nbsp; I can see a

ring on



<P>those torn fingers which I know well."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Saying this, Babalatchi stepped carelessly forward, putting his



<P>foot as if accidentally on the hand of the corpse and pressing it



<P>into the soft mud.&nbsp; He swung his staff menacingly towards the



<P>crowd, which fell back a little.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Go away," he said sternly, "and send your women to their cooking



<P>fires, which they ought not to have left to run after a dead



<P>stranger.&nbsp; This is men's work here.&nbsp; I take him now in the

name



<P>of the Rajah.&nbsp; Let no man remain here but Tuan Almayer's slaves.



<P>Now go!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The crowd reluctantly began to disperse.&nbsp; The women went first,



<P>dragging away the children that hung back with all their weight



<P>on the maternal hand.&nbsp; The men strolled slowly after them in ever



<P>forming and changing groups that gradually dissolved as they



<P>neared the settlement and every man regained his own house with



<P>steps quickened by the hungry anticipation of the morning rice.



<P>Only on the slight elevation where the land sloped down towards



<P>the muddy point a few men, either friends or enemies of Mahmat,



<P>remained gazing curiously for some time longer at the small group



<P>standing around the body on the river bank.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I do not understand what you mean, Babalatchi," said Almayer.



<P>"What is the ring you are talking about?&nbsp; Whoever he is, you have



<P>trodden the poor fellow's hand right into the mud.&nbsp; Uncover his



<P>face," he went on, addressing Mrs. Almayer, who, squatting by the



<P>head of the corpse, rocked herself to and fro, shaking from time



<P>to time her dishevelled grey locks, and muttering mournfully.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Hai!' exclaimed Mahmat, who had lingered close by.&nbsp; "Look, Tuan;



<P>the logs came together so," and here he pressed the palms of his



<P>hands together, "and his head must have been between them, and



<P>now there is no face for you to look at.&nbsp; There are his flesh and



<P>his bones, the nose, and the lips, and maybe his eyes, but nobody



<P>could tell the one from the other.&nbsp; It was written the day he was



<P>born that no man could look at him in death and be able to say,



<P>'This is my friend's face.'"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Silence, Mahmat; enough!" said Babalatchi, "and take thy eyes



<P>off his anklet, thou eater of pigs flesh.&nbsp; Tuan Almayer," he went



<P>on, lowering his voice, "have you seen Dain this morning?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer opened his eyes wide and looked alarmed.&nbsp; "No," he said



<P>quickly; "haven't you seen him?&nbsp; Is he not with the Rajah?&nbsp;

I am



<P>waiting; why does he not come?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi nodded his head sadly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"He is come, Tuan.&nbsp; He left last night when the storm was great



<P>and the river spoke angrily.&nbsp; The night was very black, but he



<P>had within him a light that showed the way to your house as



<P>smooth as a narrow backwater, and the many logs no bigger than



<P>wisps of dried grass.&nbsp; Therefore he went; and now he lies here."



<P>And Babalatchi nodded his head towards the body.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"How can you tell?" said Almayer, excitedly, pushing his wife



<P>aside.&nbsp; He snatched the cover off and looked at the formless mass



<P>of flesh, hair, and drying mud, where the face of the drowned man



<P>should have been.&nbsp; "Nobody can tell," he added, turning away with



<P>a shudder.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi was on his knees wiping the mud from the stiffened



<P>fingers of the outstretched hand.&nbsp; He rose to his feet and



<P>flashed before Almayer's eyes a gold ring set with a large green



<P>stone.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You know this well," he said.&nbsp; "This never left Dain's hand.&nbsp;

I



<P>had to tear the flesh now to get it off.&nbsp; Do you believe now?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer raised his hands to his head and let them fall listlessly



<P>by his side in the utter abandonment of despair.&nbsp; Babalatchi,



<P>looking at him curiously, was astonished to see him smile.&nbsp; A



<P>strange fancy had taken possession of Almayer's brain, distracted



<P>by this new misfortune.&nbsp; It seemed to him that for many years he



<P>had been falling into a deep precipice.&nbsp; Day after day, month



<P>after month, year after year, he had been falling, falling,



<P>falling; it was a smooth, round, black thing, and the black walls



<P>had been rushing upwards with wearisome rapidity.&nbsp; A great rush,



<P>the noise of which he fancied he could hear yet; and now, with an



<P>awful shock, he had reached the bottom, and behold! he was alive



<P>and whole, and Dain was dead with all his bones broken.&nbsp; It



<P>struck him as funny.&nbsp; A dead Malay; he had seen many dead Malays



<P>without any emotion; and now he felt inclined to weep, but it was



<P>over the fate of a white man he knew; a man that fell over a deep



<P>precipice and did not die.&nbsp; He seemed somehow to himself to be



<P>standing on one side, a little way off, looking at a certain



<P>Almayer who was in great trouble.&nbsp; Poor, poor fellow! Why doesn't



<P>he cut his throat? He wished to encourage him; he was very



<P>anxious to see him lying dead over that other corpse.&nbsp; Why does



<P>he not die and end this suffering? He groaned aloud unconsciously



<P>and started with affright at the sound of his own voice.&nbsp; Was he



<P>going mad? Terrified by the thought he turned away and ran



<P>towards his house repeating to himself, I am not going mad; of



<P>course not, no, no, no! He tried to keep a firm hold of the idea.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Not mad, not mad.&nbsp; He stumbled as he ran blindly up the steps



<P>repeating fast and ever faster those words wherein seemed to lie



<P>his salvation.&nbsp; He saw Nina standing there, and wished to say



<P>something to her, but could not remember what, in his extreme



<P>anxiety not to forget that he was not going mad, which he still



<P>kept repeating mentally as he ran round the table, till he



<P>stumbled against one of the arm-chairs and dropped into it



<P>exhausted.&nbsp; He sat staring wildly at Nina, still assuring himself



<P>mentally of his own sanity and wondering why the girl shrank from



<P>him in open-eyed alarm.&nbsp; What was the matter with her?&nbsp; This

was



<P>foolish.&nbsp; He struck the table violently with his clenched fist



<P>and shouted hoarsely, "Give me some gin!&nbsp; Run!"&nbsp; Then, while

Nina



<P>ran off, he remained in the chair, very still and quiet,



<P>astonished at the noise he had made.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Nina returned with a tumbler half filled with gin, and found her



<P>father staring absently before him.&nbsp; Almayer felt very tired now,



<P>as if he had come from a long journey.&nbsp; He felt as if he had



<P>walked miles and miles that morning and now wanted to rest very



<P>much.&nbsp; He took the tumbler with a shaking hand, and as he drank



<P>his teeth chattered against the glass which he drained and set



<P>down heavily on the table.&nbsp; He turned his eyes slowly towards



<P>Nina standing beside him, and said steadily--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Now all is over, Nina.&nbsp; He is dead, and I may as well burn all



<P>my boats."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He felt very proud of being able to speak so calmly.&nbsp; Decidedly



<P>he was not going mad.&nbsp; This certitude was very comforting, and

he



<P>went on talking about the finding of the body, listening to his



<P>own voice complacently.&nbsp; Nina stood quietly, her hand resting



<P>lightly on her father's shoulder, her face unmoved, but every



<P>line of her features, the attitude of her whole body expressing



<P>the most keen and anxious attention.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And so Dain is dead," she said coldly, when her father ceased



<P>speaking.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer's elaborately calm demeanour gave way in a moment to an



<P>outburst of violent indignation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You stand there as if you were only half alive, and talk to me,"



<P>he exclaimed angrily, "as if it was a matter of no importance.



<P>Yes, he is dead!&nbsp; Do you understand?&nbsp; Dead!&nbsp; What do

you care?



<P>You never cared; you saw me struggle, and work, and strive,



<P>unmoved; and my suffering you could never see.&nbsp; No, never.&nbsp;

You



<P>have no heart, and you have no mind, or you would have understood



<P>that it was for you, for your happiness I was working.&nbsp; I wanted



<P>to be rich; I wanted to get away from here.&nbsp; I wanted to see



<P>white men bowing low before the power of your beauty and your



<P>wealth.&nbsp; Old as I am I wished to seek a strange land, a



<P>civilisation to which I am a stranger, so as to find a new life



<P>in the contemplation of your high fortunes, of your triumphs, of



<P>your happiness.&nbsp; For that I bore patiently the burden of work,

of



<P>disappointment, of humiliation amongst these savages here, and I



<P>had it all nearly in my grasp."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He looked at his daughter's attentive face and jumped to his feet



<P>upsetting the chair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Do you hear? I had it all there; so; within reach of my hand."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He paused, trying to keep down his rising anger, and failed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Have you no feeling?" he went on.&nbsp; "Have you lived without



<P>hope?"&nbsp; Nina's silence exasperated him; his voice rose, although



<P>he tried to master his feelings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Are you content to live in this misery and die in this wretched



<P>hole?&nbsp; Say something, Nina; have you no sympathy?&nbsp; Have you

no



<P>word of comfort for me?&nbsp; I that loved you so."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He waited for a while for an answer, and receiving none shook his



<P>fist in his daughter's face.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I believe you are an idiot!" he yelled.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He looked round for the chair, picked it up and sat down stiffly.



<P>His anger was dead within him, and he felt ashamed of his



<P>outburst, yet relieved to think that now he had laid clear before



<P>his daughter the inner meaning of his life.&nbsp; He thought so in



<P>perfect good faith, deceived by the emotional estimate of his



<P>motives, unable to see the crookedness of his ways, the unreality



<P>of his aims, the futility of his regrets.&nbsp; And now his heart was



<P>filled only with a great tenderness and love for his daughter.



<P>He wanted to see her miserable, and to share with her his



<P>despair; but he wanted it only as all weak natures long for a



<P>companionship in misfortune with beings innocent of its cause.



<P>If she suffered herself she would understand and pity him; but



<P>now she would not, or could not, find one word of comfort or love



<P>for him in his dire extremity.&nbsp; The sense of his absolute



<P>loneliness came home to his heart with a force that made him



<P>shudder.&nbsp; He swayed and fell forward with his face on the table,



<P>his arms stretched straight out, extended and rigid.&nbsp; Nina made

a



<P>quick movement towards her father and stood looking at the grey



<P>head, on the broad shoulders shaken convulsively by the violence



<P>of feelings that found relief at last in sobs and tears.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Nina sighed deeply and moved away from the table.&nbsp; Her features



<P>lost the appearance of stony indifference that had exasperated



<P>her father into his outburst of anger and sorrow.&nbsp; The expression



<P>of her face, now unseen by her father, underwent a rapid change.



<P>She had listened to Almayer's appeal for sympathy, for one word



<P>of comfort, apparently indifferent, yet with her breast torn by



<P>conflicting impulses raised unexpectedly by events she had not



<P>foreseen, or at least did not expect to happen so soon.&nbsp; With her



<P>heart deeply moved by the sight of Almayer's misery, knowing it



<P>in her power to end it with a word, longing to bring peace to



<P>that troubled heart, she heard with terror the voice of her



<P>overpowering love commanding her to be silent.&nbsp; And she submitted



<P>after a short and fierce struggle of her old self against the new



<P>principle of her life.&nbsp; She wrapped herself up in absolute



<P>silence, the only safeguard against some fatal admission.&nbsp; She



<P>could not trust herself to make a sign, to murmur a word for fear



<P>of saying too much; and the very violence of the feelings that



<P>stirred the innermost recesses of her soul seemed to turn her



<P>person into a stone.&nbsp; The dilated nostrils and the flashing eyes



<P>were the only signs of the storm raging within, and those signs



<P>of his daughter's emotion Almayer did not see, for his sight was



<P>dimmed by self-pity, by anger, and by despair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Had Almayer looked at his daughter as she leant over the front



<P>rail of the verandah he could have seen the expression of



<P>indifference give way to a look of pain, and that again pass



<P>away, leaving the glorious beauty of her face marred by



<P>deep-drawn lines of watchful anxiety.&nbsp; The long grass in the



<P>neglected courtyard stood very straight before her eyes in the



<P>noonday heat.&nbsp; From the river-bank there were voices and a



<P>shuffle of bare feet approaching the house; Babalatchi could be



<P>heard giving directions to Almayer's men, and Mrs. Almayer's



<P>subdued wailing became audible as the small procession bearing



<P>the body of the drowned man and headed by that sorrowful matron



<P>turned the corner of the house.&nbsp; Babalatchi had taken the broken



<P>anklet off the man's leg, and now held it in his hand as he moved



<P>by the side of the bearers, while Mahmat lingered behind timidly,



<P>in the hopes of the promised reward.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Lay him there," said Babalatchi to Almayer's men, pointing to a



<P>pile of drying planks in front of the verandah.&nbsp; "Lay him there.



<P>He was a Kaffir and the son of a dog, and he was the white man's



<P>friend.&nbsp; He drank the white man's strong water," he added, with



<P>affected horror.&nbsp; "That I have seen myself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The men stretched out the broken limbs on two planks they had



<P>laid level, while Mrs. Almayer covered the body with a piece of



<P>white cotton cloth, and after whispering for some time with



<P>Babalatchi departed to her domestic duties.&nbsp; Almayer's men, after



<P>laying down their burden, dispersed themselves in quest of shady



<P>spots wherein to idle the day away.&nbsp; Babalatchi was left alone

by



<P>the corpse that laid rigid under the white cloth in the bright



<P>sunshine.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Nina came down the steps and joined Babalatchi, who put his hand



<P>to his forehead, and squatted down with great deference.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You have a bangle there," said Nina, looking down on



<P>Babalatchi's upturned face and into his solitary eye.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I have, Mem Putih," returned the polite statesman.&nbsp; Then turning



<P>towards Mahmat he beckoned him closer, calling out, "Come here!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mahmat approached with some hesitation.&nbsp; He avoided looking at



<P>Nina, but fixed his eyes on Babalatchi.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Now, listen," said Babalatchi, sharply.&nbsp; "The ring and the



<P>anklet you have seen, and you know they belonged to Dain the



<P>trader, and to no other.&nbsp; Dain returned last night in a canoe.



<P>He spoke with the Rajah, and in the middle of the night left to



<P>cross over to the white man's house.&nbsp; There was a great flood,



<P>and this morning you found him in the river."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"By his feet I dragged him out," muttered Mahmat under his



<P>breath.&nbsp; "Tuan Babalatchi, there will be a recompense!" he



<P>exclaimed aloud.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi held up the gold bangle before Mahmat's eyes.&nbsp; "What

I



<P>have told you, Mahmat, is for all ears.&nbsp; What I give you now is



<P>for your eyes only.&nbsp; Take."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mahmat took the bangle eagerly and hid it in the folds of his



<P>waist-cloth.&nbsp; "Am I a fool to show this thing in a house with



<P>three women in it?" he growled.&nbsp; "But I shall tell them about



<P>Dain the trader, and there will be talk enough."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He turned and went away, increasing his pace as soon as he was



<P>outside Almayer's compound.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi looked after him till he disappeared behind the



<P>bushes.&nbsp; "Have I done well, Mem Putih?" he asked, humbly



<P>addressing Nina.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You have," answered Nina.&nbsp; "The ring you may keep yourself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi touched his lips and forehead, and scrambled to his



<P>feet.&nbsp; He looked at Nina, as if expecting her to say something



<P>more, but Nina turned towards the house and went up the steps,



<P>motioning him away with her hand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi picked up his staff and prepared to go.&nbsp; It was very



<P>warm, and he did not care for the long pull to the Rajah's house.



<P>Yet he must go and tell the Rajah--tell of the event; of the



<P>change in his plans; of all his suspicions.&nbsp; He walked to the



<P>jetty and began casting off the rattan painter of his canoe.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The broad expanse of the lower reach, with its shimmering surface



<P>dotted by the black specks of the fishing canoes, lay before his



<P>eyes.&nbsp; The fishermen seemed to be racing.&nbsp; Babalatchi paused

in



<P>his work, and looked on with sudden interest.&nbsp; The man in the



<P>foremost canoe, now within hail of the first houses of Sambir,



<P>laid in his paddle and stood up shouting--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"The boats! the boats! The man-of-war's boats are coming!&nbsp; They



<P>are here!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In a moment the settlement was again alive with people rushing to



<P>the riverside.&nbsp; The men began to unfasten their boats, the women



<P>stood in groups looking towards the bend down the river.&nbsp; Above



<P>the trees lining the reach a slight puff of smoke appeared like a



<P>black stain on the brilliant blue of the cloudless sky.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi stood perplexed, the painter in his hand.&nbsp; He looked



<P>down the reach, then up towards Almayer's house, and back again



<P>at the river as if undecided what to do.&nbsp; At last he made the



<P>canoe fast again hastily, and ran towards the house and up the



<P>steps of the verandah.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Tuan! Tuan!" he called, eagerly.&nbsp; "The boats are coming.&nbsp;

The



<P>man-of-war's boats.&nbsp; You had better get ready.&nbsp; The officers

will



<P>come here, I know."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer lifted his head slowly from the table, and looked at him



<P>stupidly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Mem Putih!" exclaimed Babalatchi to Nina, "look at him.&nbsp; He does



<P>not hear.&nbsp; You must take care," he added meaningly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Nina nodded to him with an uncertain smile, and was going to



<P>speak, when a sharp report from the gun mounted in the bow of the



<P>steam launch that was just then coming into view arrested the



<P>words on her parted lips.&nbsp; The smile died out, and was replaced



<P>by the old look of anxious attention.&nbsp; From the hills far away



<P>the echo came back like a long-drawn and mournful sigh, as if the



<P>land had sent it in answer to the voice of its masters.



<P>
