<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XI.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>In the middle of a shadowless square of moonlight, shining on a



<P>smooth and level expanse of young rice-shoots, a little



<P>shelter-hut perched on high posts, the pile of brushwood near by



<P>and the glowing embers of a fire with a man stretched before it,



<P>seemed very small and as if lost in the pale green iridescence



<P>reflected from the ground.&nbsp; On three sides of the clearing,



<P>appearing very far away in the deceptive light, the big trees of



<P>the forest, lashed together with manifold bonds by a mass of



<P>tangled creepers, looked down at the growing young life at their



<P>feet with the sombre resignation of giants that had lost faith in



<P>their strength.&nbsp; And in the midst of them the merciless creepers



<P>clung to the big trunks in cable-like coils, leaped from tree to



<P>tree, hung in thorny festoons from the lower boughs, and, sending



<P>slender tendrils on high to seek out the smallest branches,



<P>carried death to their victims in an exulting riot of silent



<P>destruction.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>On the fourth side, following the curve of the bank of that



<P>branch of the Pantai that formed the only access to the clearing,



<P>ran a black line of young trees, bushes, and thick second growth,



<P>unbroken save for a small gap chopped out in one place.&nbsp; At that



<P>gap began the narrow footpath leading from the water's edge to



<P>the grass-built shelter used by the night watchers when the



<P>ripening crop had to be protected from the wild pigs.&nbsp; The



<P>pathway ended at the foot of the piles on which the hut was



<P>built, in a circular space covered with ashes and bits of burnt



<P>wood.&nbsp; In the middle of that space, by the dim fire, lay Dain.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He turned over on his side with an impatient sigh, and, pillowing



<P>his head on his bent arm, lay quietly with his face to the dying



<P>fire.&nbsp; The glowing embers shone redly in a small circle, throwing



<P>a gleam into his wide-open eyes, and at every deep breath the



<P>fine white ash of bygone fires rose in a light cloud before his



<P>parted lips, and danced away from the warm glow into the



<P>moonbeams pouring down upon Bulangi's clearing.&nbsp; His body was



<P>weary with the exertion of the past few days, his mind more weary



<P>still with the strain of solitary waiting for his fate.&nbsp; Never



<P>before had he felt so helpless.&nbsp; He had heard the report of the



<P>gun fired on board the launch, and he knew that his life was in



<P>untrustworthy hands, and that his enemies were very near.&nbsp; During



<P>the slow hours of the afternoon he roamed about on the edge of



<P>the forest, or, hiding in the bushes, watched the creek with



<P>unquiet eyes for some sign of danger.&nbsp; He feared not death, yet



<P>he desired ardently to live, for life to him was Nina.&nbsp; She had



<P>promised to come, to follow him, to share his danger and his



<P>splendour.&nbsp; But with her by his side he cared not for danger, and



<P>without her there could be no splendour and no joy in existence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Crouching in his shady hiding-place, he closed his eyes, trying



<P>to evoke the gracious and charming image of the white figure that



<P>for him was the beginning and the end of life.&nbsp; With eyes shut



<P>tight, his teeth hard set, he tried in a great effort of



<P>passionate will to keep his hold on that vision of supreme



<P>delight.&nbsp; In vain!&nbsp; His heart grew heavy as the figure of

Nina



<P>faded away to be replaced by another vision this time--a vision



<P>of armed men, of angry faces, of glittering arms--and he seemed



<P>to hear the hum of excited and triumphant voices as they



<P>discovered him in his hiding-place.&nbsp; Startled by the vividness

of



<P>his fancy, he would open his eyes, and, leaping out into the



<P>sunlight, resume his aimless wanderings around the clearing.&nbsp; As



<P>he skirted in his weary march the edge of the forest he glanced



<P>now and then into its dark shade, so enticing in its deceptive



<P>appearance of coolness, so repellent with its unrelieved gloom,



<P>where lay, entombed and rotting, countless generations of trees,



<P>and where their successors stood as if mourning, in dark green



<P>foliage, immense and helpless, awaiting their turn.&nbsp; Only the



<P>parasites seemed to live there in a sinuous rush upwards into the



<P>air and sunshine, feeding on the dead and the dying alike, and



<P>crowning their victims with pink and blue flowers that gleamed



<P>amongst the boughs, incongruous and cruel, like a strident and



<P>mocking note in the solemn harmony of the doomed trees.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A man could hide there, thought Dain, as he approached a place



<P>where the creepers had been torn and hacked into an archway that



<P>might have been the beginning of a path.&nbsp; As he bent down to look



<P>through he heard angry grunting, and a sounder of wild pig



<P>crashed away in the undergrowth.&nbsp; An acrid smell of damp earth



<P>and of decaying leaves took him by the throat, and he drew back



<P>with a scared face, as if he had been touched by the breath of



<P>Death itself.&nbsp; The very air seemed dead in there--heavy and



<P>stagnating, poisoned with the corruption of countless ages.&nbsp; He



<P>went on, staggering on his way, urged by the nervous restlessness



<P>that made him feel tired yet caused him to loathe the very idea



<P>of immobility and repose.&nbsp; Was he a wild man to hide in the woods



<P>and perhaps be killed there--in the darkness--where there was no



<P>room to breathe?&nbsp; He would wait for his enemies in the sunlight,



<P>where he could see the sky and feel the breeze.&nbsp; He knew how a



<P>Malay chief should die.&nbsp; The sombre and desperate fury, that



<P>peculiar inheritance of his race, took possession of him, and he



<P>glared savagely across the clearing towards the gap in the bushes



<P>by the riverside.&nbsp; They would come from there.&nbsp; In imagination

he



<P>saw them now.&nbsp; He saw the bearded faces and the white jackets of



<P>the officers, the light on the levelled barrels of the rifles.



<P>What is the bravery of the greatest warrior before the firearms



<P>in the hand of a slave?&nbsp; He would walk toward them with a smiling



<P>face, with his hands held out in a sign of submission till he was



<P>very near them.&nbsp; He would speak friendly words--come nearer



<P>yet--yet nearer--so near that they could touch him with their



<P>hands and stretch them out to make him a captive.&nbsp; That would be



<P>the time:&nbsp; with a shout and a leap he would be in the midst of



<P>them, kriss in hand, killing, killing, killing, and would die



<P>with the shouts of his enemies in his ears, their warm blood



<P>spurting before his eyes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Carried away by his excitement, he snatched the kriss hidden in



<P>his sarong, and, drawing a long breath, rushed forward, struck at



<P>the empty air, and fell on his face.&nbsp; He lay as if stunned in the



<P>sudden reaction from his exaltation, thinking that, even if he



<P>died thus gloriously, it would have to be before he saw Nina.



<P>Better so.&nbsp; If he saw her again he felt that death would be too



<P>terrible.&nbsp; With horror he, the descendant of Rajahs and of



<P>conquerors, had to face the doubt of his own bravery.&nbsp; His desire



<P>of life tormented him in a paroxysm of agonising remorse.&nbsp; He had



<P>not the courage to stir a limb.&nbsp; He had lost faith in himself,



<P>and there was nothing else in him of what makes a man.&nbsp; The



<P>suffering remained, for it is ordered that it should abide in the



<P>human body even to the last breath, and fear remained.&nbsp; Dimly he



<P>could look into the depths of his passionate love, see its



<P>strength and its weakness, and felt afraid.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The sun went down slowly.&nbsp; The shadow of the western forest



<P>marched over the clearing, covered the man's scorched shoulders



<P>with its cool mantle, and went on hurriedly to mingle with the



<P>shadows of other forests on the eastern side.&nbsp; The sun lingered



<P>for a while amongst the light tracery of the higher branches, as



<P>if in friendly reluctance to abandon the body stretched in the



<P>green paddy-field.&nbsp; Then Dain, revived by the cool of the evening



<P>breeze, sat up and stared round him.&nbsp; As he did so the sun dipped



<P>sharply, as if ashamed of being detected in a sympathising



<P>attitude, and the clearing, which during the day was all light,



<P>became suddenly all darkness, where the fire gleamed like an eye.



<P>Dain walked slowly towards the creek, and, divesting himself of



<P>his torn sarong, his only garment, entered the water cautiously.



<P>He had had nothing to eat that day, and had not dared show



<P>himself in daylight by the water-side to drink.&nbsp; Now, as he swam



<P>silently, he swallowed a few mouthfuls of water that lapped about



<P>his lips.&nbsp; This did him good, and he walked with greater



<P>confidence in himself and others as he returned towards the fire.



<P>Had he been betrayed by Lakamba all would have been over by this.



<P>He made up a big blaze, and while it lasted dried himself, and



<P>then lay down by the embers.&nbsp; He could not sleep, but he felt a



<P>great numbness in all his limbs.&nbsp; His restlessness was gone, and



<P>he was content to lay still, measuring the time by watching the



<P>stars that rose in endless succession above the forests, while



<P>the slight puffs of wind under the cloudless sky seemed to fan



<P>their twinkle into a greater brightness.&nbsp; Dreamily he assured



<P>himself over and over again that she would come, till the



<P>certitude crept into his heart and filled him with a great peace.



<P>Yes, when the next day broke, they would be together on the great



<P>blue sea that was like life--away from the forests that were like



<P>death.&nbsp; He murmured the name of Nina into the silent space with

a



<P>tender smile: this seemed to break the spell of stillness, and



<P>far away by the creek a frog croaked loudly as if in answer.&nbsp; A



<P>chorus of loud roars and plaintive calls rose from the mud along



<P>the line of bushes.&nbsp; He laughed heartily; doubtless it was their



<P>love-song.&nbsp; He felt affectionate towards the frogs and listened,



<P>pleased with the noisy life near him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When the moon peeped above the trees he felt the old impatience



<P>and the old restlessness steal over him.&nbsp; Why was she so late?



<P>True, it was a long way to come with a single paddle.&nbsp; With what



<P>skill and what endurance could those small hands manage a heavy



<P>paddle!&nbsp; It was very wonderful--such small hands, such soft



<P>little palms that knew how to touch his cheek with a feel lighter



<P>than the fanning of a butterfly's wing.&nbsp; Wonderful!&nbsp; He lost



<P>himself lovingly in the contemplation of this tremendous mystery,



<P>and when he looked at the moon again it had risen a hand's



<P>breadth above the trees.&nbsp; Would she come?&nbsp; He forced himself

to



<P>lay still, overcoming the impulse to rise and rush round the



<P>clearing again.&nbsp; He turned this way and that; at last, quivering



<P>with the effort, he lay on his back, and saw her face among the



<P>stars looking down on him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The croaking of frogs suddenly ceased.&nbsp; With the watchfulness of



<P>a hunted man Dain sat up, listening anxiously, and heard several



<P>splashes in the water as the frogs took rapid headers into the



<P>creek.&nbsp; He knew that they had been alarmed by something, and



<P>stood up suspicious and attentive.&nbsp; A slight grating noise, then



<P>the dry sound as of two pieces of wood struck against each other.



<P>Somebody was about to land!&nbsp; He took up an armful of brushwood,



<P>and, without taking his eyes from the path, held it over the



<P>embers of his fire.&nbsp; He waited, undecided, and saw something



<P>gleam amongst the bushes; then a white figure came out of the



<P>shadows and seemed to float towards him in the pale light.&nbsp; His



<P>heart gave a great leap and stood still, then went on shaking his



<P>frame in furious beats.&nbsp; He dropped the brushwood upon the



<P>glowing coals, and had an impression of shouting her name--of



<P>rushing to meet her; yet he emitted no sound, he stirred not an



<P>inch, but he stood silent and motionless like chiselled bronze



<P>under the moonlight that streamed over his naked shoulders.&nbsp; As



<P>he stood still, fighting with his breath, as if bereft of his



<P>senses by the intensity of his delight, she walked up to him with



<P>quick, resolute steps, and, with the appearance of one about to



<P>leap from a dangerous height, threw both her arms round his neck



<P>with a sudden gesture.&nbsp; A small blue gleam crept amongst the dry



<P>branches, and the crackling of reviving fire was the only sound



<P>as they faced each other in the speechless emotion of that



<P>meeting; then the dry fuel caught at once, and a bright hot flame



<P>shot upwards in a blaze as high as their heads, and in its light



<P>they saw each other's eyes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Neither of them spoke.&nbsp; He was regaining his senses in a slight



<P>tremor that ran upwards along his rigid body and hung about his



<P>trembling lips.&nbsp; She drew back her head and fastened her eyes on



<P>his in one of those long looks that are a woman's most terrible



<P>weapon; a look that is more stirring than the closest touch, and



<P>more dangerous than the thrust of a dagger, because it also whips



<P>the soul out of the body, but leaves the body alive and helpless,



<P>to be swayed here and there by the capricious tempests of passion



<P>and desire; a look that enwraps the whole body, and that



<P>penetrates into the innermost recesses of the being, bringing



<P>terrible defeat in the delirious uplifting of accomplished



<P>conquest.&nbsp; It has the same meaning for the man of the forests and



<P>the sea as for the man threading the paths of the more dangerous



<P>wilderness of houses and streets.&nbsp; Men that had felt in their



<P>breasts the awful exultation such a look awakens become mere



<P>things of to-day--which is paradise; forget yesterday--which was



<P>suffering; care not for to-morrow--which may be perdition.&nbsp; They



<P>wish to live under that look for ever.&nbsp; It is the look of woman's



<P>surrender.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He understood, and, as if suddenly released from his invisible



<P>bonds, fell at her feet with a shout of joy, and, embracing her



<P>knees, hid his head in the folds of her dress, murmuring



<P>disjointed words of gratitude and love.&nbsp; Never before had he felt



<P>so proud as now, when at the feet of that woman that half



<P>belonged to his enemies.&nbsp; Her fingers played with his hair in an



<P>absent-minded caress as she stood absorbed in thought.&nbsp; The thing



<P>was done.&nbsp; Her mother was right.&nbsp; The man was her slave.&nbsp;

As she



<P>glanced down at his kneeling form she felt a great pitying



<P>tenderness for that man she was used to call--even in her



<P>thoughts--the master of life.&nbsp; She lifted her eyes and looked



<P>sadly at the southern heavens under which lay the path of their



<P>lives--her own, and that man's at her feet.&nbsp; Did he not say



<P>himself is that she was the light of his life?&nbsp; She would be his



<P>light and his wisdom; she would be his greatness and his



<P>strength; yet hidden from the eyes of all men she would be, above



<P>all, his only and lasting weakness.&nbsp; A very woman!&nbsp; In the



<P>sublime vanity of her kind she was thinking already of moulding a



<P>god from the clay at her feet.&nbsp; A god for others to worship.&nbsp;

She



<P>was content to see him as he was now, and to feel him quiver at



<P>the slightest touch of her light fingers.&nbsp; And while her eyes



<P>looked sadly at the southern stars a faint smile seemed to be



<P>playing about her firm lips.&nbsp; Who can tell in the fitful light

of



<P>a camp fire?&nbsp; It might have been a smile of triumph, or of



<P>conscious power, or of tender pity, or, perhaps, of love.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She spoke softly to him, and he rose to his feet, putting his arm



<P>round her in quiet consciousness of his ownership; she laid her



<P>head on his shoulder with a sense of defiance to all the world in



<P>the encircling protection of that arm.&nbsp; He was hers with all his



<P>qualities and all his faults.&nbsp; His strength and his courage, his



<P>recklessness and his daring, his simple wisdom and his savage



<P>cunning--all were hers.&nbsp; As they passed together out of the red



<P>light of the fire into the silver shower of rays that fell upon



<P>the clearing he bent his head over her face, and she saw in his



<P>eyes the dreamy intoxication of boundless felicity from the close



<P>touch of her slight figure clasped to his side.&nbsp; With a



<P>rhythmical swing of their bodies they walked through the light



<P>towards the outlying shadows of the forests that seemed to guard



<P>their happiness in solemn immobility.&nbsp; Their forms melted in the



<P>play of light and shadow at the foot of the big trees, but the



<P>murmur of tender words lingered over the empty clearing, grew



<P>faint, and died out.&nbsp; A sigh as of immense sorrow passed over the



<P>land in the last effort of the dying breeze, and in the deep



<P>silence which succeeded, the earth and the heavens were suddenly



<P>hushed up in the mournful contemplation of human love and human



<P>blindness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They walked slowly back to the fire.&nbsp; He made for her a seat out



<P>of the dry branches, and, throwing himself down at her feet, lay



<P>his head in her lap and gave himself up to the dreamy delight of



<P>the passing hour.&nbsp; Their voices rose and fell, tender or animated



<P>as they spoke of their love and of their future.&nbsp; She, with a few



<P>skilful words spoken from time to time, guided his thoughts, and



<P>he let his happiness flow in a stream of talk passionate and



<P>tender, grave or menacing, according to the mood which she



<P>evoked.&nbsp; He spoke to her of his own island, where the gloomy



<P>forests and the muddy rivers were unknown.&nbsp; He spoke of its



<P>terraced fields, of the murmuring clear rills of sparkling water



<P>that flowed down the sides of great mountains, bringing life to



<P>the land and joy to its tillers.&nbsp; And he spoke also of the



<P>mountain peak that rising lonely above the belt of trees knew the



<P>secrets of the passing clouds, and was the dwelling-place of the



<P>mysterious spirit of his race, of the guardian genius of his



<P>house.&nbsp; He spoke of vast horizons swept by fierce winds that



<P>whistled high above the summits of burning mountains.&nbsp; He spoke



<P>of his forefathers that conquered ages ago the island of which he



<P>was to be the future ruler.&nbsp; And then as, in her interest, she



<P>brought her face nearer to his, he, touching lightly the thick



<P>tresses of her long hair, felt a sudden impulse to speak to her



<P>of the sea he loved so well; and he told her of its never-ceasing



<P>voice, to which he had listened as a child, wondering at its



<P>hidden meaning that no living man has penetrated yet; of its



<P>enchanting glitter; of its senseless and capricious fury; how its



<P>surface was for ever changing, and yet always enticing, while its



<P>depths were for ever the same, cold and cruel, and full of the



<P>wisdom of destroyed life.&nbsp; He told her how it held men slaves of



<P>its charm for a lifetime, and then, regardless of their devotion,



<P>swallowed them up, angry at their fear of its mystery, which it



<P>would never disclose, not even to those that loved it most.



<P>While he talked, Nina's head had been gradually sinking lower,



<P>and her face almost touched his now.&nbsp; Her hair was over his eyes,



<P>her breath was on his forehead, her arms were about his body.&nbsp;

No



<P>two beings could be closer to each other, yet she guessed rather



<P>than understood the meaning of his last words that came out after



<P>a slight hesitation in a faint murmur, dying out imperceptibly



<P>into a profound and significant silence:&nbsp; "The sea, O Nina, is



<P>like a woman's heart."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She closed his lips with a sudden kiss, and answered in a steady



<P>voice--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But to the men that have no fear, O master of my life, the sea



<P>is ever true."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Over their heads a film of dark, thread-like clouds, looking like



<P>immense cobwebs drifting under the stars, darkened the sky with



<P>the presage of the coming thunderstorm.&nbsp; From the invisible hills



<P>the first distant rumble of thunder came in a prolonged roll



<P>which, after tossing about from hill to hill, lost itself in the



<P>forests of the Pantai.&nbsp; Dain and Nina stood up, and the former



<P>looked at the sky uneasily.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It is time for Babalatchi to be here," he said.&nbsp; "The night is



<P>more than half gone.&nbsp; Our road is long, and a bullet travels



<P>quicker than the best canoe."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"He will be here before the moon is hidden behind the clouds,"



<P>said Nina.&nbsp; "I heard a splash in the water," she added.&nbsp; "Did

you



<P>hear it too?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Alligator," answered Dain shortly, with a careless glance



<P>towards the creek.&nbsp; "The darker the night," he continued, "the



<P>shorter will be our road, for then we could keep in the current



<P>of the main stream, but if it is light--even no more than now--we



<P>must follow the small channels of sleeping water, with nothing to



<P>help our paddles."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Dain," interposed Nina, earnestly, "it was no alligator.&nbsp; I



<P>heard the bushes rustling near the landing-place."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes," said Dain, after listening awhile.&nbsp; "It cannot be



<P>Babalatchi, who would come in a big war canoe, and openly.&nbsp; Those



<P>that are coming, whoever they are, do not wish to make much



<P>noise.&nbsp; But you have heard, and now I can see," he went on



<P>quickly.&nbsp; "It is but one man.&nbsp; Stand behind me, Nina.&nbsp;

If he is a



<P>friend he is welcome; if he is an enemy you shall see him die."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He laid his hand on his kriss, and awaited the approach of his



<P>unexpected visitor.&nbsp; The fire was burning very low, and small



<P>clouds--precursors of the storm--crossed the face of the moon in



<P>rapid succession, and their flying shadows darkened the clearing.



<P>He could not make out who the man might be, but he felt uneasy at



<P>the steady advance of the tall figure walking on the path with a



<P>heavy tread, and hailed it with a command to stop.&nbsp; The man



<P>stopped at some little distance, and Dain expected him to speak,



<P>but all he could hear was his deep breathing.&nbsp; Through a break

in



<P>the flying clouds a sudden and fleeting brightness descended upon



<P>the clearing.&nbsp; Before the darkness closed in again, Dain saw a



<P>hand holding some glittering object extended towards him, heard



<P>Nina's cry of "Father!" and in an instant the girl was between



<P>him and Almayer's revolver.&nbsp; Nina's loud cry woke up the echoes



<P>of the sleeping woods, and the three stood still as if waiting



<P>for the return of silence before they would give expression to



<P>their various feelings.&nbsp; At the appearance of Nina, Almayer's arm



<P>fell by his side, and he made a step forward.&nbsp; Dain pushed the



<P>girl gently aside.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Am I a wild beast that you should try to kill me suddenly and in



<P>the dark, Tuan Almayer?" said Dain, breaking the strained



<P>silence.&nbsp; "Throw some brushwood on the fire," he went on,



<P>speaking to Nina, "while I watch my white friend, lest harm



<P>should come to you or to me, O delight of my heart!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer ground his teeth and raised his arm again.&nbsp; With a quick



<P>bound Dain was at his side: there was a short scuffle, during



<P>which one chamber of the revolver went off harmlessly, then the



<P>weapon, wrenched out of Almayer's hand, whirled through the air



<P>and fell in the bushes.&nbsp; The two men stood close together,



<P>breathing hard.&nbsp; The replenished fire threw out an unsteady



<P>circle of light and shone on the terrified face of Nina, who



<P>looked at them with outstretched hands.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Dain!" she cried out warningly, "Dain!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He waved his hand towards her in a reassuring gesture, and,



<P>turning to Almayer, said with great courtesy--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Now we may talk, Tuan.&nbsp; It is easy to send out death, but can



<P>your wisdom recall the life?&nbsp; She might have been harmed," he



<P>continued, indicating Nina.&nbsp; "Your hand shook much; for myself

I



<P>was not afraid."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Nina!" exclaimed Almayer, "come to me at once.&nbsp; What is this



<P>sudden madness?&nbsp; What bewitched you?&nbsp; Come to your father,

and



<P>together we shall try to forget this horrible nightmare!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He opened his arms with the certitude of clasping her to his



<P>breast in another second.&nbsp; She did not move.&nbsp; As it dawned

upon



<P>him that she did not mean to obey he felt a deadly cold creep



<P>into his heart, and, pressing the palms of his hands to his



<P>temples, he looked down on the ground in mute despair.&nbsp; Dain took



<P>Nina by the arm and led her towards her father.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Speak to him in the language of his people," he said.&nbsp; "He is



<P>grieving--as who would not grieve at losing thee, my pearl!



<P>Speak to him the last words he shall hear spoken by that voice,



<P>which must be very sweet to him, but is all my life to me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He released her, and, stepping back a few paces out of the circle



<P>of light, stood in the darkness looking at them with calm



<P>interest.&nbsp; The reflection of a distant flash of lightning lit up



<P>the clouds over their heads, and was followed after a short



<P>interval by the faint rumble of thunder, which mingled with



<P>Almayer's voice as he began to speak.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Do you know what you are doing?&nbsp; Do you know what is waiting for



<P>you if you follow that man?&nbsp; Have you no pity for yourself?&nbsp;

Do



<P>you know that you shall be at first his plaything and then a



<P>scorned slave, a drudge, and a servant of some new fancy of that



<P>man?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She raised her hand to stop him, and turning her head slightly,



<P>asked--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You hear this Dain!&nbsp; Is it true?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"By all the gods!" came the impassioned answer from the



<P>darkness-- "by heaven and earth, by my head and thine I swear:



<P>this is a white man's lie.&nbsp; I have delivered my soul into your



<P>hands for ever; I breathe with your breath, I see with your eyes,



<P>I think with your mind, and I take you into my heart for ever."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You thief!" shouted the exasperated Almayer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A deep silence succeeded this outburst, then the voice of Dain



<P>was heard again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Nay, Tuan," he said in a gentle tone, "that is not true also.



<P>The girl came of her own will.&nbsp; I have done no more but to show



<P>her my love like a man; she heard the cry of my heart, and she



<P>came, and the dowry I have given to the woman you call your



<P>wife."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer groaned in his extremity of rage and shame.&nbsp; Nina laid



<P>her hand lightly on his shoulder, and the contact, light as the



<P>touch of a falling leaf, seemed to calm him.&nbsp; He spoke quickly,



<P>and in English this time.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Tell me," he said--"tell me, what have they done to you, your



<P>mother and that man?&nbsp; What made you give yourself up to that



<P>savage?&nbsp; For he is a savage.&nbsp; Between him and you there is

a



<P>barrier that nothing can remove.&nbsp; I can see in your eyes the look



<P>of those who commit suicide when they are mad.&nbsp; You are mad.



<P>Don't smile.&nbsp; It breaks my heart.&nbsp; If I were to see you drowning



<P>before my eyes, and I without the power to help you, I could not



<P>suffer a greater torment.&nbsp; Have you forgotten the teaching of so



<P>many years?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No," she interrupted, "I remember it well.&nbsp; I remember how it



<P>ended also.&nbsp; Scorn for scorn, contempt for contempt, hate for



<P>hate.&nbsp; I am not of your race.&nbsp; Between your people and me

there



<P>is also a barrier that nothing can remove.&nbsp; You ask why I want

to



<P>go, and I ask you why I should stay."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He staggered as if struck in the face, but with a quick,



<P>unhesitating grasp she caught him by the arm and steadied him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Why you should stay!" he repeated slowly, in a dazed manner, and



<P>stopped short, astounded at the completeness of his misfortune.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You told me yesterday," she went on again, "that I could not



<P>understand or see your love for me:&nbsp; it is so.&nbsp; How can I?&nbsp;

No



<P>two human beings understand each other.&nbsp; They can understand but



<P>their own voices.&nbsp; You wanted me to dream your dreams, to see



<P>your own visions--the visions of life amongst the white faces of



<P>those who cast me out from their midst in angry contempt.&nbsp; But



<P>while you spoke I listened to the voice of my own self; then this



<P>man came, and all was still; there was only the murmur of his



<P>love.&nbsp; You call him a savage!&nbsp; What do you call my mother,

your



<P>wife?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Nina!" cried Almayer, "take your eyes off my face."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She looked down directly, but continued speaking only a little



<P>above a whisper.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"In time," she went on, "both our voices, that man's and mine,



<P>spoke together in a sweetness that was intelligible to our ears



<P>only.&nbsp; You were speaking of gold then, but our ears were filled



<P>with the song of our love, and we did not hear you.&nbsp; Then I found



<P>that we could see through each other's eyes:&nbsp; that he saw things



<P>that nobody but myself and he could see.&nbsp; We entered a land where



<P>no one could follow us, and least of all you.&nbsp; Then I began to



<P>live."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She paused.&nbsp; Almayer sighed deeply.&nbsp; With her eyes still fixed

on



<P>the ground she began speaking again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And I mean to live.&nbsp; I mean to follow him.&nbsp; I have been rejected



<P>with scorn by the white people, and now I am a Malay!&nbsp; He took

me



<P>in his arms, he laid his life at my feet.&nbsp; He is brave; he will



<P>be powerful, and I hold his bravery and his strength in my hand,



<P>and I shall make him great.&nbsp; His name shall be remembered long



<P>after both our bodies are laid in the dust.&nbsp; I love you no less



<P>than I did before, but I shall never leave him, for without him I



<P>cannot live."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"If he understood what you have said," answered Almayer,



<P>scornfully, "he must be highly flattered.&nbsp; You want him as a tool



<P>for some incomprehensible ambition of yours.&nbsp; Enough, Nina.&nbsp;

If



<P>you do not go down at once to the creek, where Ali is waiting



<P>with my canoe, I shall tell him to return to the settlement and



<P>bring the Dutch officers here.&nbsp; You cannot escape from this



<P>clearing, for I have cast adrift your canoe.&nbsp; If the Dutch catch



<P>this hero of yours they will hang him as sure as I stand here.



<P>Now go."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He made a step towards his daughter and laid hold of her by the



<P>shoulder, his other hand pointing down the path to the



<P>landing-place.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Beware!" exclaimed Dain; "this woman belongs to me!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Nina wrenched herself free and looked straight at Almayer's angry



<P>face.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No, I will not go," she said with desperate energy.&nbsp; "If he dies



<P>I shall die too!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You die!" said Almayer, contemptuously.&nbsp; "Oh, no!&nbsp; You shall



<P>live a life of lies and deception till some other vagabond comes



<P>along to sing; how did you say that?&nbsp; The song of love to you!



<P>Make up your mind quickly."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He waited for a while, and then added meaningly--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Shall I call out to Ali?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Call out," she answered in Malay, "you that cannot be true to



<P>your own countrymen.&nbsp; Only a few days ago you were selling the



<P>powder for their destruction; now you want to give up to them the



<P>man that yesterday you called your friend.&nbsp; Oh, Dain," she said,



<P>turning towards the motionless but attentive figure in the



<P>darkness, "instead of bringing you life I bring you death, for he



<P>will betray unless I leave you for ever!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Dain came into the circle of light, and, throwing his arm around



<P>Nina's neck, whispered in her ear--"I can kill him where he



<P>stands, before a sound can pass his lips.&nbsp; For you it is to say



<P>yes or no.&nbsp; Babalatchi cannot be far now."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He straightened himself up, taking his arm off her shoulder, and



<P>confronted Almayer, who looked at them both with an expression of



<P>concentrated fury,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No!" she cried, clinging to Dain in wild alarm.&nbsp; "No!&nbsp; Kill

me!



<P>Then perhaps he will let you go.&nbsp; You do not know the mind of a



<P>white man.&nbsp; He would rather see me dead than standing where I am.



<P>Forgive me, your slave, but you must not."&nbsp; She fell at his feet



<P>sobbing violently and repeating, "Kill me!&nbsp; Kill me!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I want you alive," said Almayer, speaking also in Malay, with



<P>sombre calmness.&nbsp; "You go, or he hangs.&nbsp; Will you obey?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Dain shook Nina off, and, making a sudden lunge, struck Almayer



<P>full in the chest with the handle of his kriss, keeping the point



<P>towards himself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Hai, look! It was easy for me to turn the point the other way,"



<P>he said in his even voice.&nbsp; "Go, Tuan Putih," he added with



<P>dignity.&nbsp; "I give you your life, my life, and her life.&nbsp; I

am the



<P>slave of this woman's desire, and she wills it so."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was not a glimmer of light in the sky now, and the tops of



<P>the trees were as invisible as their trunks, being lost in the



<P>mass of clouds that hung low over the woods, the clearing, and



<P>the river.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Every outline had disappeared in the intense blackness that



<P>seemed to have destroyed everything but space.&nbsp; Only the fire



<P>glimmered like a star forgotten in this annihilation of all



<P>visible things, and nothing was heard after Dain ceased speaking



<P>but the sobs of Nina, whom he held in his arms, kneeling beside



<P>the fire.&nbsp; Almayer stood looking down at them in gloomy



<P>thoughtfulness.&nbsp; As he was opening his lips to speak they were



<P>startled by a cry of warning by the riverside, followed by the



<P>splash of many paddles and the sound of voices.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Babalatchi!" shouted Dain, lifting up Nina as he got upon his



<P>feet quickly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Ada!&nbsp; Ada!" came the answer from the panting statesman who ran



<P>up the path and stood amongst them.&nbsp; "Run to my canoe," he said



<P>to Dain excitedly, without taking any notice of Almayer.&nbsp; "Run!



<P>we must go.&nbsp; That woman has told them all!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What woman?" asked Dain, looking at Nina.&nbsp; Just then there was



<P>only one woman in the whole world for him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"The she-dog with white teeth; the seven times accursed slave of



<P>Bulangi.&nbsp; She yelled at Abdulla's gate till she woke up all



<P>Sambir.&nbsp; Now the white officers are coming, guided by her and



<P>Reshid.&nbsp; If you want to live, do not look at me, but go!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"How do you know this?" asked Almayer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, Tuan! what matters how I know!&nbsp; I have only one eye, but I



<P>saw lights in Abdulla's house and in his campong as we were



<P>paddling past.&nbsp; I have ears, and while we lay under the bank I



<P>have heard the messengers sent out to the white men's house."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Will you depart without that woman who is my daughter?" said



<P>Almayer, addressing Dain, while Babalatchi stamped with



<P>impatience, muttering, "Run!&nbsp; Run at once!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No," answered Dain, steadily, "I will not go; to no man will I



<P>abandon this woman."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Then kill me and escape yourself," sobbed out Nina.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He clasped her close, looking at her tenderly, and whispered, "We



<P>will never part, O Nina!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I shall not stay here any longer," broke in Babalatchi, angrily.



<P>"This is great foolishness.&nbsp; No woman is worth a man's life.&nbsp;

I



<P>am an old man, and I know."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He picked up his staff, and, turning to go, looked at Dain as if



<P>offering him his last chance of escape.&nbsp; But Dain's face was



<P>hidden amongst Nina's black tresses, and he did not see this last



<P>appealing glance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Babalatchi vanished in the darkness.&nbsp; Shortly after his



<P>disappearance they heard the war canoe leave the landing-place in



<P>the swish of the numerous paddles dipped in the water together.



<P>Almost at the same time Ali came up from the riverside, two



<P>paddles on his shoulder.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Our canoe is hidden up the creek, Tuan Almayer," he said, "in



<P>the dense bush where the forest comes down to the water.&nbsp; I took



<P>it there because I heard from Babalatchi's paddlers that the



<P>white men are coming here."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Wait for me there," said Almayer, "but keep the canoe hidden."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He remained silent, listening to Ali's footsteps, then turned to



<P>Nina.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Nina," he said sadly, "will you have no pity for me?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was no answer.&nbsp; She did not even turn her head, which was



<P>pressed close to Dain's breast.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He made a movement as if to leave them and stopped.&nbsp; By the dim



<P>glow of the burning-out fire he saw their two motionless figures.



<P>The woman's back turned to him with the long black hair streaming



<P>down over the white dress, and Dain's calm face looking at him



<P>above her head.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I cannot," he muttered to himself.&nbsp; After a long pause he spoke



<P>again a little lower, but in an unsteady voice, "It would be too



<P>great a disgrace.&nbsp; I am a white man."&nbsp; He broke down completely



<P>there, and went on tearfully, "I am a white man, and of good



<P>family.&nbsp; Very good family," he repeated, weeping bitterly.&nbsp;

"It



<P>would be a disgrace . . .&nbsp; all over the islands, . . . the only



<P>white man on the east coast.&nbsp; No, it cannot be . . . white men



<P>finding my daughter with this Malay.&nbsp; My daughter!" he cried



<P>aloud, with a ring of despair in his voice.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He recovered his composure after a while and said distinctly--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I will never forgive you, Nina--never! If you were to come back



<P>to me now, the memory of this night would poison all my life.&nbsp;

I



<P>shall try to forget.&nbsp; I have no daughter.&nbsp; There used to be

a



<P>half-caste woman in my house, but she is going even now.&nbsp; You,



<P>Dain, or whatever your name may be, I shall take you and that



<P>woman to the island at the mouth of the river myself.&nbsp; Come with



<P>me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He led the way, following the bank as far as the forest.&nbsp; Ali



<P>answered to his call, and, pushing their way through the dense



<P>bush, they stepped into the canoe hidden under the overhanging



<P>branches.&nbsp; Dain laid Nina in the bottom, and sat holding her head



<P>on his knees.&nbsp; Almayer and Ali each took up a paddle.&nbsp; As

they



<P>were going to push out Ali hissed warningly.&nbsp; All listened.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In the great stillness before the bursting out of the



<P>thunderstorm they could hear the sound of oars working regularly



<P>in their row-locks.&nbsp; The sound approached steadily, and Dain,



<P>looking through the branches, could see the faint shape of a big



<P>white boat.&nbsp; A woman's voice said in a cautious tone--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"There is the place where you may land white men; a little higher



<P>--there!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The boat was passing them so close in the narrow creek that the



<P>blades of the long oars nearly touched the canoe.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Way enough! Stand by to jump on shore!&nbsp; He is alone and



<P>unarmed," was the quiet order in a man's voice, and in Dutch.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Somebody else whispered: "I think I can see a glimmer of a fire



<P>through the bush."&nbsp; And then the boat floated past them,



<P>disappearing instantly in the darkness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Now," whispered Ali, eagerly, "let us push out and paddle away."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The little canoe swung into the stream, and as it sprung forward



<P>in response to the vigorous dig of the paddles they could hear an



<P>angry shout.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"He is not by the fire.&nbsp; Spread out, men, and search for him!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Blue lights blazed out in different parts of the clearing, and



<P>the shrill voice of a woman cried in accents of rage and pain--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Too late!&nbsp; O senseless white men!&nbsp; He has escaped!"



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER V.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>At last the excitement had died out in Sambir.&nbsp; The inhabitants



<P>got used to the sight of comings and goings between Almayer's



<P>house and the vessel, now moored to the opposite bank, and



<P>speculation as to the feverish activity displayed by Almayer's



<P>boatmen in repairing old canoes ceased to interfere with the due



<P>discharge of domestic duties by the women of the Settlement.



<P>Even the baffled Jim-Eng left off troubling his muddled brain



<P>with secrets of trade, and relapsed by the aid of his opium pipe



<P>into a state of stupefied bliss, letting Babalatchi pursue his



<P>way past his house uninvited and seemingly unnoticed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>So on that warm afternoon, when the deserted river sparkled under



<P>the vertical sun, the statesman of Sambir could, without any



<P>hindrance from friendly inquirers, shove off his little canoe



<P>from under the bushes, where it was usually hidden during his



<P>visits to Almayer's compound.&nbsp; Slowly and languidly Babalatchi



<P>paddled, crouching low in the boat, making himself small under



<P>his as enormous sun hat to escape the scorching heat reflected



<P>from the water.&nbsp; He was not in a hurry; his master, Lakamba, was



<P>surely reposing at this time of the day.&nbsp; He would have ample



<P>time to cross over and greet him on his waking with important



<P>news.&nbsp; Will he be displeased?&nbsp; Will he strike his ebony wood



<P>staff angrily on the floor, frightening him by the incoherent



<P>violence of his exclamations; or will he squat down with a



<P>good-humoured smile, and, rubbing his hands gently over his



<P>stomach with a familiar gesture, expectorate copiously into the



<P>brass siri-vessel, giving vent to a low, approbative murmur?



<P>Such were Babalatchi's thoughts as he skilfully handled his



<P>paddle, crossing the river on his way to the Rajah's campong,



<P>whose stockades showed from behind the dense foliage of the bank



<P>just opposite to Almayer's bungalow.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Indeed, he had a report to make.&nbsp; Something certain at last to



<P>confirm the daily tale of suspicions, the daily hints of



<P>familiarity, of stolen glances he had seen, of short and burning



<P>words he had overheard exchanged between Dain Maroola and



<P>Almayer's daughter.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lakamba had, till then, listened to it all, calmly and with



<P>evident distrust; now he was going to be convinced, for



<P>Babalatchi had the proof; had it this very morning, when fishing



<P>at break of day in the creek over which stood Bulangi's house.



<P>There from his skiff he saw Nina's long canoe drift past, the



<P>girl sitting in the stern bending over Dain, who was stretched in



<P>the bottom with his head resting on the girl's knees.&nbsp; He saw it.



<P>He followed them, but in a short time they took to the paddles



<P>and got away from under his observant eye.&nbsp; A few minutes



<P>afterwards he saw Bulangi's slave-girl paddling in a small



<P>dug-out to the town with her cakes for sale.&nbsp; She also had seen



<P>them in the grey dawn.&nbsp; And Babalatchi grinned confidentially to



<P>himself at the recollection of the slave-girl's discomposed face,



<P>of the hard look in her eyes, of the tremble in her voice, when



<P>answering his questions.&nbsp; That little Taminah evidently admired



<P>Dain Maroola.&nbsp; That was good!&nbsp; And Babalatchi laughed aloud

at



<P>the notion; then becoming suddenly serious, he began by some



<P>strange association of ideas to speculate upon the price for



<P>which Bulangi would, possibly, sell the girl.&nbsp; He shook his head



<P>sadly at the thought that Bulangi was a hard man, and had refused



<P>one hundred dollars for that same Taminah only a few weeks ago;



<P>then he became suddenly aware that the canoe had drifted too far



<P>down during his meditation.&nbsp; He shook off the despondency caused



<P>by the certitude of Bulangi's mercenary disposition, and, taking



<P>up his paddle, in a few strokes sheered alongside the water-gate



<P>of the Rajah's house.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>That afternoon Almayer, as was his wont lately, moved about on



<P>the water-side, overlooking the repairs to his boats.&nbsp; He had



<P>decided at last.&nbsp; Guided by the scraps of information contained



<P>in old Lingard's pocket-book, he was going to seek for the rich



<P>gold-mine, for that place where he had only to stoop to gather up



<P>an immense fortune and realise the dream of his young days.&nbsp; To



<P>obtain the necessary help he had shared his knowledge with Dain



<P>Maroola, he had consented to be reconciled with Lakamba, who gave



<P>his support to the enterprise on condition of sharing the



<P>profits; he had sacrificed his pride, his honour, and his loyalty



<P>in the face of the enormous risk of his undertaking, dazzled by



<P>the greatness of the results to be achieved by this alliance so



<P>distasteful yet so necessary.&nbsp; The dangers were great, but



<P>Maroola was brave; his men seemed as reckless as their chief, and



<P>with Lakamba's aid success seemed assured.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For the last fortnight Almayer was absorbed in the preparations,



<P>walking amongst his workmen and slaves in a kind of waking



<P>trance, where practical details as to the fitting out of the



<P>boats were mixed up with vivid dreams of untold wealth, where the



<P>present misery of burning sun, of the muddy and malodorous river



<P>bank disappeared in a gorgeous vision of a splendid future



<P>existence for himself and Nina.&nbsp;&nbsp; He hardly saw Nina during

these



<P>last days, although the beloved daughter was ever present in his



<P>thoughts.&nbsp; He hardly took notice of Dain, whose constant presence



<P>in his house had become a matter of course to him now they were



<P>connected by a community of interests.&nbsp;&nbsp; When meeting the

young



<P>chief he gave him an absent greeting and passed on, seemingly



<P>wishing to avoid him, bent upon forgetting the hated reality of



<P>the present by absorbing himself in his work, or else by letting



<P>his imagination soar far above the tree-tops into the great white



<P>clouds away to the westward, where the paradise of Europe was



<P>awaiting the future Eastern millionaire.&nbsp; And Maroola, now the



<P>bargain was struck and there was no more business to be talked



<P>over, evidently did not care for the white man's company.&nbsp; Yet



<P>Dain was always about the house, but he seldom stayed long by the



<P>riverside.&nbsp; On his daily visits to the white man the Malay chief



<P>preferred to make his way quietly through the central passage of



<P>the house, and would come out into the garden at the back, where



<P>the fire was burning in the cooking shed, with the rice kettle



<P>swinging over it, under the watchful supervision of Mrs. Almayer.



<P>Avoiding that shed, with its black smoke and the warbling of



<P>soft, feminine voices, Dain would turn to the left.&nbsp; There, on



<P>the edge of a banana plantation, a clump of palms and mango trees



<P>formed a shady spot, a few scattered bushes giving it a certain



<P>seclusion into which only the serving women's chatter or an



<P>occasional burst of laughter could penetrate.&nbsp; Once in, he was



<P>invisible; and hidden there, leaning against the smooth trunk of



<P>a tall palm, he waited with gleaming eyes and an assured smile to



<P>hear the faint rustle of dried grass under the light footsteps of



<P>Nina.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>From the very first moment when his eyes beheld this--to him--



<P>perfection of loveliness he felt in his inmost heart the



<P>conviction that she would be his; he felt the subtle breath of



<P>mutual understanding passing between their two savage natures,



<P>and he did not want Mrs. Almayer's encouraging smiles to take



<P>every opportunity of approaching the girl; and every time he



<P>spoke to her, every time he looked into her eyes, Nina, although



<P>averting her face, felt as if this bold-looking being who spoke



<P>burning words into her willing ear was the embodiment of her



<P>fate, the creature of her dreams--reckless, ferocious, ready with



<P>flashing kriss for his enemies, and with passionate embrace for



<P>his beloved--the ideal Malay chief of her mother's tradition.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She recognised with a thrill of delicious fear the mysterious



<P>consciousness of her identity with that being.&nbsp; Listening to his



<P>words, it seemed to her she was born only then to a knowledge of



<P>a new existence, that her life was complete only when near him,



<P>and she abandoned herself to a feeling of dreamy happiness, while



<P>with half- veiled face and in silence--as became a Malay



<P>girl--she listened to Dain's words giving up to her the whole



<P>treasure of love and passion his nature was capable of with all



<P>the unrestrained enthusiasm of a man totally untrammelled by any



<P>influence of civilised self-discipline.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And they used to pass many a delicious and fast fleeting hour



<P>under the mango trees behind the friendly curtain of bushes till



<P>Mrs. Almayer's shrill voice gave the signal of unwilling



<P>separation.&nbsp; Mrs. Almayer had undertaken the easy task of



<P>watching her husband lest he should interrupt the smooth course



<P>of her daughter's love affair, in which she took a great and



<P>benignant interest.&nbsp; She was happy and proud to see Dain's



<P>infatuation, believing him to be a great and powerful chief, and



<P>she found also a gratification of her mercenary instincts in



<P>Dain's open-handed generosity.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>On the eve of the day when Babalatchi's suspicions were confirmed



<P>by ocular demonstration, Dain and Nina had remained longer than



<P>usual in their shady retreat.&nbsp; Only Almayer's heavy step on the



<P>verandah and his querulous clamour for food decided Mrs. Almayer



<P>to lift a warning cry.&nbsp; Maroola leaped lightly over the low



<P>bamboo fence, and made his way stealthily through the banana



<P>plantation down to the muddy shore of the back creek, while Nina



<P>walked slowly towards the house to minister to her father's



<P>wants, as was her wont every evening.&nbsp;&nbsp; Almayer felt happy

enough



<P>that evening; the preparations were nearly completed; to-morrow



<P>he would launch his boats.&nbsp; In his mind's eye he saw the rich



<P>prize in his grasp; and, with tin spoon in his hand, he was



<P>forgetting the plateful of rice before him in the fanciful



<P>arrangement of some splendid banquet to take place on his arrival



<P>in Amsterdam.&nbsp; Nina, reclining in the long chair, listened



<P>absently to the few disconnected words escaping from her father's



<P>lips.&nbsp; Expedition!&nbsp; Gold! What did she care for all that?&nbsp;

But at



<P>the name of Maroola mentioned by her father she was all



<P>attention.&nbsp; Dain was going down the river with his brig to-morrow



<P>to remain away for a few days, said Almayer.&nbsp; It was very



<P>annoying, this delay.&nbsp; As soon as Dain returned they would have



<P>to start without loss of time, for the river was rising.&nbsp; He



<P>would not be surprised if a great flood was coming.&nbsp; And he



<P>pushed away his plate with an impatient gesture on rising from



<P>the table.&nbsp; But now Nina heard him not.&nbsp; Dain going away!&nbsp;

That's



<P>why he had ordered her, with that quiet masterfulness it was her



<P>delight to obey, to meet him at break of day in Bulangi's creek.



<P>Was there a paddle in her canoe? she thought.&nbsp; Was it ready?&nbsp;

She



<P>would have to start early--at four in the morning, in a very few



<P>hours.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She rose from her chair, thinking she would require rest before



<P>the long pull in the early morning.&nbsp; The lamp was burning dimly,



<P>and her father, tired with the day's labour, was already in his



<P>hammock.&nbsp; Nina put the lamp out and passed into a large room she



<P>shared with her mother on the left of the central passage.



<P>Entering, she saw that Mrs. Almayer had deserted the pile of mats



<P>serving her as bed in one corner of the room, and was now bending



<P>over the opened lid of her large wooden chest.&nbsp; Half a shell of



<P>cocoanut filled with oil, where a cotton rag floated for a wick,



<P>stood on the floor, surrounding her with a ruddy halo of light



<P>shining through the black and odorous smoke.&nbsp; Mrs. Almayer's back



<P>was bent, and her head and shoulders hidden in the deep box.&nbsp; Her



<P>hands rummaged in the interior, where a soft clink as of silver



<P>money could be heard.&nbsp; She did not notice at first her daughter's



<P>approach, and Nina, standing silently by her, looked down on many



<P>little canvas bags ranged in the bottom of the chest, wherefrom



<P>her mother extracted handfuls of shining guilders and Mexican



<P>dollars, letting them stream slowly back again through her



<P>claw-like fingers.&nbsp; The music of tinkling silver seemed to



<P>delight her, and her eyes sparkled with the reflected gleam of



<P>freshly-minted coins.&nbsp; She was muttering to herself: "And this,



<P>and this, and yet this!&nbsp; Soon he will give more--as much more as



<P>I ask.&nbsp; He is a great Rajah--a Son of Heaven!&nbsp; And she will

be a



<P>Ranee--he gave all this for her!&nbsp; Who ever gave anything for me?



<P>I am a slave!&nbsp; Am I?&nbsp; I am the mother of a great Ranee!" She



<P>became aware suddenly of her daughter's presence, and ceased her



<P>droning, shutting the lid down violently; then, without rising



<P>from her crouching position, she looked up at the girl standing



<P>by with a vague smile on her dreamy face.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You have seen.&nbsp; Have you?" she shouted, shrilly.&nbsp; "That is

all



<P>mine, and for you.&nbsp; It is not enough!&nbsp; He will have to give

more



<P>before he takes you away to the southern island where his father



<P>is king.&nbsp; You hear me?&nbsp; You are worth more, granddaughter

of



<P>Rajahs!&nbsp; More!&nbsp; More!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The sleepy voice of Almayer was heard on the verandah



<P>recommending silence.&nbsp; Mrs. Almayer extinguished the light and



<P>crept into her corner of the room.&nbsp; Nina laid down on her back

on



<P>a pile of soft mats, her hands entwined under her head, gazing



<P>through the shutterless hole, serving as a window at the stars



<P>twinkling on the black sky; she was awaiting the time of start



<P>for her appointed meeting-place.&nbsp; With quiet happiness she



<P>thought of that meeting in the great forest, far from all human



<P>eyes and sounds.&nbsp; Her soul, lapsing again into the savage mood,



<P>which the genius of civilisation working by the hand of Mrs.



<P>Vinck could never destroy, experienced a feeling of pride and of



<P>some slight trouble at the high value her worldly-wise mother had



<P>put upon her person; but she remembered the expressive glances



<P>and words of Dain, and, tranquillised, she closed her eyes in a



<P>shiver of pleasant anticipation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There are some situations where the barbarian and the, so-called,



<P>civilised man meet upon the same ground.&nbsp; It may be supposed that



<P>Dain Maroola was not exceptionally delighted with his prospective



<P>mother-in-law, nor that he actually approved of that worthy



<P>woman's appetite for shining dollars.&nbsp; Yet on that foggy morning



<P>when Babalatchi, laying aside the cares of state, went to visit



<P>his fish-baskets in the Bulangi creek, Maroola had no misgivings,



<P>experienced no feelings but those of impatience and longing, when



<P>paddling to the east side of the island forming the back-water in



<P>question.&nbsp; He hid his canoe in the bushes and strode rapidly



<P>across the islet, pushing with impatience through the twigs of



<P>heavy undergrowth intercrossed over his path.&nbsp; From motives of



<P>prudence he would not take his canoe to the meeting-place, as



<P>Nina had done.&nbsp; He had left it in the main stream till his return



<P>from the other side of the island.&nbsp; The heavy warm fog was



<P>closing rapidly round him, but he managed to catch a fleeting



<P>glimpse of a light away to the left, proceeding from Bulangi's



<P>house.&nbsp; Then he could see nothing in the thickening vapour, and



<P>kept to the path only by a sort of instinct, which also led him



<P>to the very point on the opposite shore he wished to reach.&nbsp; A



<P>great log had stranded there, at right angles to the bank,



<P>forming a kind of jetty against which the swiftly flowing stream



<P>broke with a loud ripple.&nbsp; He stepped on it with a quick but



<P>steady motion, and in two strides found himself at the outer end,



<P>with the rush and swirl of the foaming water at his feet.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Standing there alone, as if separated from the world; the



<P>heavens, earth; the very water roaring under him swallowed up in



<P>the thick veil of the morning fog, he breathed out the name of



<P>Nina before him into the apparently limitless space, sure of



<P>being heard, instinctively sure of the nearness of the delightful



<P>creature; certain of her being aware of his near presence as he



<P>was aware of hers.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The bow of Nina's canoe loomed up close to the log, canted high



<P>out of the water by the weight of the sitter in the stern.



<P>Maroola laid his hand on the stem and leaped lightly in, giving



<P>it a vigorous shove off.&nbsp; The light craft, obeying the new



<P>impulse, cleared the log by a hair's breadth, and the river, with



<P>obedient complicity, swung it broadside to the current, and bore



<P>it off silently and rapidly between the invisible banks.&nbsp; And



<P>once more Dain, at the feet of Nina, forgot the world, felt



<P>himself carried away helpless by a great wave of supreme emotion,



<P>by a rush of joy, pride, and desire; understood once more with



<P>overpowering certitude that there was no life possible without



<P>that being he held clasped in his arms with passionate strength



<P>in a prolonged embrace.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Nina disengaged herself gently with a low laugh.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You will overturn the boat, Dain," she whispered.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He looked into her eyes eagerly for a minute and let her go with



<P>a sigh, then lying down in the canoe he put his head on her



<P>knees, gazing upwards and stretching his arms backwards till his



<P>hands met round the girl's waist.&nbsp; She bent over him, and,



<P>shaking her head, framed both their faces in the falling locks of



<P>her long black hair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And so they drifted on, he speaking with all the rude eloquence



<P>of a savage nature giving itself up without restraint to an



<P>overmastering passion, she bending low to catch the murmur of



<P>words sweeter to her than life itself.&nbsp; To those two nothing



<P>existed then outside the gunwales of the narrow and fragile



<P>craft.&nbsp; It was their world, filled with their intense and



<P>all-absorbing love.&nbsp; They took no heed of thickening mist, or of



<P>the breeze dying away before sunrise; they forgot the existence



<P>of the great forests surrounding them, of all the tropical nature



<P>awaiting the advent of the sun in a solemn and impressive



<P>silence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Over the low river-mist hiding the boat with its freight of young



<P>passionate life and all-forgetful happiness, the stars paled, and



<P>a silvery-grey tint crept over the sky from the eastward.&nbsp; There



<P>was not a breath of wind, not a rustle of stirring leaf, not a



<P>splash of leaping fish to disturb the serene repose of all living



<P>things on the banks of the great river.&nbsp; Earth, river, and sky



<P>were wrapped up in a deep sleep, from which it seemed there would



<P>be no waking.&nbsp; All the seething life and movement of tropical



<P>nature seemed concentrated in the ardent eyes, in the



<P>tumultuously beating hearts of the two beings drifting in the



<P>canoe, under the white canopy of mist, over the smooth surface of



<P>the river.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Suddenly a great sheaf of yellow rays shot upwards from behind



<P>the black curtain of trees lining the banks of the Pantai.&nbsp; The



<P>stars went out; the little black clouds at the zenith glowed for



<P>a moment with crimson tints, and the thick mist, stirred by the



<P>gentle breeze, the sigh of waking nature, whirled round and broke



<P>into fantastically torn pieces, disclosing the wrinkled surface



<P>of the river sparkling in the broad light of day.&nbsp; Great flocks



<P>of white birds wheeled screaming above the swaying tree-tops.



<P>The sun had risen on the east coast.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Dain was the first to return to the cares of everyday life.&nbsp; He



<P>rose and glanced rapidly up and down the river.&nbsp; His eye detected



<P>Babalatchi's boat astern, and another small black speck on the



<P>glittering water, which was Taminah's canoe.&nbsp; He moved cautiously



<P>forward, and, kneeling, took up a paddle; Nina at the stern took



<P>hers.&nbsp; They bent their bodies to the work, throwing up the water



<P>at every stroke, and the small craft went swiftly ahead, leaving



<P>a narrow wake fringed with a lace-like border of white and



<P>gleaming foam.&nbsp; Without turning his head, Dain spoke.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Somebody behind us, Nina.&nbsp; We must not let him gain.&nbsp; I think

he



<P>is too far to recognise us."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Somebody before us also," panted out Nina, without ceasing to



<P>paddle.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I think I know," rejoined Dain.&nbsp; "The sun shines over there, but



<P>I fancy it is the girl Taminah.&nbsp; She comes down every morning to



<P>my brig to sell cakes--stays often all day.&nbsp; It does not matter;



<P>steer more into the bank; we must get under the bushes.&nbsp; My canoe



<P>is hidden not far from here."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he spoke his eyes watched the broad-leaved nipas which they



<P>were brushing in their swift and silent course.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Look out, Nina," he said at last; "there, where the water palms



<P>end and the twigs hang down under the leaning tree.&nbsp; Steer for



<P>the big green branch."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He stood up attentive, and the boat drifted slowly in shore, Nina



<P>guiding it by a gentle and skilful movement of her paddle.&nbsp; When



<P>near enough Dain laid hold of the big branch, and leaning back



<P>shot the canoe under a low green archway of thickly matted



<P>creepers giving access to a miniature bay formed by the caving in



<P>of the bank during the last great flood.&nbsp; His own boat was there



<P>anchored by a stone, and he stepped into it, keeping his hand on



<P>the gunwale of Nina's canoe.&nbsp; In a moment the two little



<P>nutshells with their occupants floated quietly side by side,



<P>reflected by the black water in the dim light struggling through



<P>a high canopy of dense foliage; while above, away up in the broad



<P>day, flamed immense red blossoms sending down on their heads a



<P>shower of great dew-sparkling petals that descended rotating



<P>slowly in a continuous and perfumed stream; and over them, under



<P>them, in the sleeping water; all around them in a ring of



<P>luxuriant vegetation bathed in the warm air charged with strong



<P>and harsh perfumes, the intense work of tropical nature went on:



<P>plants shooting upward, entwined, interlaced in inextricable



<P>confusion, climbing madly and brutally over each other in the



<P>terrible silence of a desperate struggle towards the life-giving



<P>sunshine above--as if struck with sudden horror at the seething



<P>mass of corruption below, at the death and decay from which they



<P>sprang.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"We must part now," said Dain, after a long silence.&nbsp; "You must



<P>return at once, Nina.&nbsp; I will wait till the brig drifts down



<P>here, and shall get on board then."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And will you be long away, Dain?" asked Nina, in a low voice.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Long!" exclaimed Dain.&nbsp; "Would a man willingly remain long in

a



<P>dark place?&nbsp; When I am not near you, Nina, I am like a man that



<P>is blind.&nbsp; What is life to me without light?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Nina leaned over, and with a proud and happy smile took Dain's



<P>face between her hands, looking into his eyes with a fond yet



<P>questioning gaze.&nbsp; Apparently she found there the confirmation

of



<P>the words just said, for a feeling of grateful security lightened



<P>for her the weight of sorrow at the hour of parting.&nbsp; She



<P>believed that he, the descendant of many great Rajahs, the son of



<P>a great chief, the master of life and death, knew the sunshine of



<P>life only in her presence.&nbsp; An immense wave of gratitude and love



<P>welled forth out of her heart towards him.&nbsp; How could she make

an



<P>outward and visible sign of all she felt for the man who had



<P>filled her heart with so much joy and so much pride?&nbsp; And in the



<P>great tumult of passion, like a flash of lightning came to her



<P>the reminiscence of that despised and almost forgotten



<P>civilisation she had only glanced at in her days of restraint, of



<P>sorrow, and of anger.&nbsp; In the cold ashes of that hateful and



<P>miserable past she would find the sign of love, the fitting



<P>expression of the boundless felicity of the present, the pledge



<P>of a bright and splendid future.&nbsp; She threw her arms around



<P>Dain's neck and pressed her lips to his in a long and burning



<P>kiss.&nbsp; He closed his eyes, surprised and frightened at the storm



<P>raised in his breast by the strange and to him hitherto unknown



<P>contact, and long after Nina had pushed her canoe into the river



<P>he remained motionless, without daring to open his eyes, afraid



<P>to lose the sensation of intoxicating delight he had tasted for



<P>the first time.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Now he wanted but immortality, he thought, to be the equal of



<P>gods, and the creature that could open so the gates of paradise



<P>must be his--soon would be his for ever!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He opened his eyes in time to see through the archway of creepers



<P>the bows of his brig come slowly into view, as the vessel drifted



<P>past on its way down the river.&nbsp; He must go on board now, he



<P>thought; yet he was loth to leave the place where he had learned



<P>to know what happiness meant.&nbsp; "Time yet.&nbsp; Let them go," he



<P>muttered to himself; and he closed his eyes again under the red



<P>shower of scented petals, trying to recall the scene with all its



<P>delight and all its fear.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He must have been able to join his brig in time, after all, and



<P>found much occupation outside, for it was in vain that Almayer



<P>looked for his friend's speedy return.&nbsp; The lower reach of the



<P>river where he so often and so impatiently directed his eyes



<P>remained deserted, save for the rapid flitting of some fishing



<P>canoe; but down the upper reaches came black clouds and heavy



<P>showers heralding the final setting in of the rainy season with



<P>its thunderstorms and great floods making the river almost



<P>impossible of ascent for native canoes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer, strolling along the muddy beach between his houses,



<P>watched uneasily the river rising inch by inch, creeping slowly



<P>nearer to the boats, now ready and hauled up in a row under the



<P>cover of dripping Kajang-mats.&nbsp; Fortune seemed to elude his



<P>grasp, and in his weary tramp backwards and forwards under the



<P>steady rain falling from the lowering sky, a sort of despairing



<P>indifference took possession of him.&nbsp; What did it matter?&nbsp;

It was



<P>just his luck!&nbsp; Those two infernal savages, Lakamba and Dain,



<P>induced him, with their promises of help, to spend his last



<P>dollar in the fitting out of boats, and now one of them was gone



<P>somewhere, and the other shut up in his stockade would give no



<P>sign of life.&nbsp; No, not even the scoundrelly Babalatchi, thought



<P>Almayer, would show his face near him, now they had sold him all



<P>the rice, brass gongs, and cloth necessary for his expedition.



<P>They had his very last coin, and did not care whether he went or



<P>stayed.&nbsp; And with a gesture of abandoned discouragement Almayer



<P>would climb up slowly to the verandah of his new house to get out



<P>of the rain, and leaning on the front rail with his head sunk



<P>between his shoulders he would abandon himself to the current of



<P>bitter thoughts, oblivious of the flight of time and the pangs of



<P>hunger, deaf to the shrill cries of his wife calling him to the



<P>evening meal.&nbsp; When, roused from his sad meditations by the first



<P>roll of the evening thunderstorm, he stumbled slowly towards the



<P>glimmering light of his old house, his half-dead hope made his



<P>ears preternaturally acute to any sound on the river.&nbsp; Several



<P>nights in succession he had heard the splash of paddles and had



<P>seen the indistinct form of a boat, but when hailing the shadowy



<P>apparition, his heart bounding with sudden hope of hearing Dain's



<P>voice, he was disappointed each time by the sulky answer



<P>conveying to him the intelligence that the Arabs were on the



<P>river, bound on a visit to the home-staying Lakamba.&nbsp; This caused



<P>him many sleepless nights, spent in speculating upon the kind of



<P>villainy those estimable personages were hatching now.&nbsp; At last,



<P>when all hope seemed dead, he was overjoyed on hearing Dain's



<P>voice; but Dain also appeared very anxious to see Lakamba, and



<P>Almayer felt uneasy owing to a deep and ineradicable distrust as



<P>to that ruler's disposition towards himself.&nbsp; Still, Dain had



<P>returned at last.&nbsp; Evidently he meant to keep to his bargain.



<P>Hope revived, and that night Almayer slept soundly, while Nina



<P>watched the angry river under the lash of the thunderstorm



<P>sweeping onward towards the sea.



<P>
