</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter02</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER II.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>When, in compliance with Lingard's abrupt demand, Almayer



<P>consented to wed the Malay girl, no one knew that on the day when



<P>the interesting young convert had lost all her natural relations



<P>and found a white father, she had been fighting desperately like



<P>the rest of them on board the prau, and was only prevented from



<P>leaping overboard, like the few other survivors, by a severe



<P>wound in the leg.&nbsp; There, on the fore-deck of the prau, old



<P>Lingard found her under a heap of dead and dying pirates, and had



<P>her carried on the poop of the Flash before the Malay craft was



<P>set on fire and sent adrift.&nbsp; She was conscious, and in the great



<P>peace and stillness of the tropical evening succeeding the



<P>turmoil of the battle, she watched all she held dear on earth



<P>after her own savage manner, drift away into the gloom in a great



<P>roar of flame and smoke.&nbsp; She lay there unheeding the careful



<P>hands attending to her wound, silent and absorbed in gazing at



<P>the funeral pile of those brave men she had so much admired and



<P>so well helped in their contest with the redoubtable



<P>"Rajah-Laut."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The light night breeze fanned the brig gently to the southward,



<P>and the great blaze of light got smaller and smaller till it



<P>twinkled only on the horizon like a setting star.&nbsp; It set:&nbsp;

the



<P>heavy canopy of smoke reflected the glare of hidden flames for a



<P>short time and then disappeared also.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She realised that with this vanishing gleam her old life departed



<P>too.&nbsp; Thenceforth there was slavery in the far countries, amongst



<P>strangers, in unknown and perhaps terrible surroundings.&nbsp; Being



<P>fourteen years old, she realised her position and came to that



<P>conclusion, the only one possible to a Malay girl, soon ripened



<P>under a tropical sun, and not unaware of her personal charms, of



<P>which she heard many a young brave warrior of her father's crew



<P>express an appreciative admiration.&nbsp; There was in her the dread



<P>of the unknown; otherwise she accepted her position calmly, after



<P>the manner of her people, and even considered it quite natural;



<P>for was she not a daughter of warriors, conquered in battle, and



<P>did she not belong rightfully to the victorious Rajah?&nbsp; Even the



<P>evident kindness of the terrible old man must spring, she



<P>thought, from admiration for his captive, and the flattered



<P>vanity eased for her the pangs of sorrow after such an awful



<P>calamity.&nbsp; Perhaps had she known of the high walls, the quiet



<P>gardens, and the silent nuns of the Samarang convent, where her



<P>destiny was leading her, she would have sought death in her dread



<P>and hate of such a restraint.&nbsp; But in imagination she pictured

to



<P>herself the usual life of a Malay girl--the usual succession of



<P>heavy work and fierce love, of intrigues, gold ornaments, of



<P>domestic drudgery, and of that great but occult influence which



<P>is one of the few rights of half-savage womankind.&nbsp; But her



<P>destiny in the rough hands of the old sea-dog, acting under



<P>unreasoning impulses of the heart, took a strange and to her a



<P>terrible shape.&nbsp; She bore it all--the restraint and the teaching



<P>and the new faith--with calm submission, concealing her hate&nbsp; and



<P>contempt for all that new life.&nbsp; She learned the language very



<P>easily, yet understood but little of the new faith the good



<P>sisters taught her, assimilating quickly only the superstitious



<P>elements of the religion.&nbsp; She called Lingard father, gently and



<P>caressingly, at each of his short and noisy visits, under the



<P>clear impression that he was a great and dangerous power it was



<P>good to propitiate.&nbsp; Was he not now her master?&nbsp; And during

those



<P>long four years she nourished a hope of finding favour in his



<P>eyes and ultimately becoming his wife, counsellor, and guide.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Those dreams of the future were dispelled by the Rajah Laut's



<P>"fiat," which made Almayer's fortune, as that young man fondly



<P>hoped.&nbsp; And dressed in the hateful finery of Europe, the centre



<P>of an interested circle of Batavian society, the young convert



<P>stood before the altar with an unknown and sulky-looking white



<P>man.&nbsp; For Almayer was uneasy, a little disgusted, and greatly



<P>inclined to run away.&nbsp; A judicious fear of the adopted



<P>father-in-law and a just regard for his own material welfare



<P>prevented him from making a scandal; yet, while swearing



<P>fidelity, he was concocting plans for getting rid of the&nbsp; pretty



<P>Malay girl in a more or less distant future.&nbsp; She, however, had



<P>retained enough of conventual teaching to understand well that



<P>according to white men's laws she was going to be Almayer's



<P>companion and not his slave, and promised to herself to act



<P>accordingly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>So when the Flash freighted with materials for building a new



<P>house left the harbour of Batavia, taking away the young couple



<P>into the unknown Borneo, she did not carry on her deck so much



<P>love and happiness as old Lingard was wont to boast of before his



<P>casual friends in the verandahs of various hotels.&nbsp; The old



<P>seaman himself was perfectly happy.&nbsp; Now he had done his duty by



<P>the girl.&nbsp; "You know I made her an orphan," he often concluded



<P>solemnly, when talking about his own affairs to a scratch



<P>audience of shore loafers--as it was his habit to do.&nbsp; And the



<P>approbative shouts of his half-intoxicated auditors filled his



<P>simple soul with delight and pride.&nbsp; "I carry everything right



<P>through," was another of his sayings, and in pursuance of that



<P>principle he pushed the building of house and godowns on the



<P>Pantai River with feverish haste.&nbsp; The house for the young



<P>couple; the godowns for the big trade Almayer was going to



<P>develop while he (Lingard) would be able to give himself up to



<P>some mysterious work which was only spoken of in hints, but was



<P>understood to relate to gold and diamonds in the interior of the



<P>island.&nbsp; Almayer was impatient too.&nbsp; Had he known what was

before



<P>him he might not have been so eager and full of hope as he stood



<P>watching the last canoe of the Lingard expedition disappear in



<P>the bend up the river.&nbsp; When, turning round, he beheld the pretty



<P>little house, the big godowns built neatly by an army of Chinese



<P>carpenters, the new jetty round which were clustered the trading



<P>canoes, he felt a sudden elation in the thought that the world



<P>was his.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the world had to be conquered first, and its conquest was not



<P>so easy as he thought.&nbsp; He was very soon made to understand that



<P>he was not wanted in that corner of it where old Lingard and his



<P>own weak will placed him, in the midst of unscrupulous intrigues



<P>and of a fierce trade competition.&nbsp; The Arabs had found out the



<P>river, had established a trading post in Sambir, and where they



<P>traded they would be masters and suffer no rival.&nbsp; Lingard



<P>returned unsuccessful from his first expedition, and departed



<P>again spending all the profits of the legitimate trade on his



<P>mysterious journeys.&nbsp; Almayer struggled with the difficulties of



<P>his position, friendless and unaided, save for the protection



<P>given to him for Lingard's sake by the old Rajah, the predecessor



<P>of Lakamba.&nbsp; Lakamba himself, then living as a private individual



<P>on a rice clearing, seven miles down the river, exercised all his



<P>influence towards the help of the white man's enemies, plotting



<P>against the old Rajah and Almayer with a certainty of



<P>combination, pointing clearly to a profound knowledge of their



<P>most secret affairs.&nbsp; Outwardly friendly, his portly form was



<P>often to be seen on Almayer's verandah; his green turban and



<P>gold-embroidered jacket shone in the front rank of the decorous



<P>throng of Malays coming to greet Lingard on his returns from the



<P>interior; his salaams were of the lowest, and his hand-shakings



<P>of the heartiest, when welcoming the old trader.&nbsp; But his small



<P>eyes took in the signs of the times, and he departed from those



<P>interviews with a satisfied and furtive smile to hold long



<P>consultations with his friend and ally, Syed Abdulla, the&nbsp; chief



<P>of the Arab trading post, a man of great wealth and of great



<P>influence in the islands.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was currently believed at that time in the settlement that



<P>Lakamba's visits to Almayer's house were not limited to those



<P>official interviews.&nbsp; Often on moonlight nights the belated



<P>fishermen of Sambira saw a small canoe shooting out from the



<P>narrow creek at the back of the white man's house, and the



<P>solitary occupant paddle cautiously down the river in the deep



<P>shadows of the bank; and those events, duly reported, were



<P>discussed round the evening fires far into the night with the



<P>cynicism of expression common to aristocratic Malays, and with a



<P>malicious pleasure in the domestic misfortunes of the Orang



<P>Blando--the hated Dutchman.&nbsp; Almayer went on struggling



<P>desperately, but with a feebleness of purpose depriving him of



<P>all chance of success against men so unscrupulous and resolute as



<P>his rivals the Arabs.&nbsp; The trade fell away from the large



<P>godowns, and the godowns themselves rotted piecemeal.&nbsp; The old



<P>man's banker, Hudig of Macassar, failed, and with this went the



<P>whole available capital.&nbsp; The profits of past years had been



<P>swallowed up in Lingard's exploring craze.&nbsp; Lingard was in the



<P>interior--perhaps dead--at all events giving no sign of life.



<P>Almayer stood alone in the midst of those adverse circumstances,



<P>deriving only a little comfort from the companionship of his



<P>little daughter, born two years after the marriage, and at the



<P>time some six years old.&nbsp; His wife had soon commenced to treat



<P>him with a savage contempt expressed by sulky silence, only



<P>occasionally varied by a flood of savage invective.&nbsp; He felt she



<P>hated him, and saw her jealous eyes watching himself and the



<P>child with almost an expression of hate.&nbsp; She was jealous of the



<P>little girl's evident preference for the father, and Almayer felt



<P>he was not safe with that woman in the house.&nbsp; While she was



<P>burning the furniture, and tearing down the pretty curtains in



<P>her unreasoning hate of those signs of civilisation, Almayer,



<P>cowed by these outbursts of savage nature, meditated in silence



<P>on the best way of getting rid of her.&nbsp; He thought of everything;



<P>even planned murder in an undecided and feeble sort of way, but



<P>dared do nothing--expecting every day the return of Lingard with



<P>news of some immense good fortune.&nbsp; He returned indeed, but aged,



<P>ill, a ghost of his former self, with the fire of fever burning



<P>in his sunken eyes, almost the only survivor of the numerous



<P>expedition.&nbsp; But he was successful at last!&nbsp; Untold riches

were



<P>in his grasp; he wanted more money--only a little more torealise



<P>a dream of fabulous fortune.&nbsp; And Hudig had failed!&nbsp; Almayer



<P>scraped all he could together, but the old man wanted more.&nbsp; If



<P>Almayer could not get it he would go to Singapore--to Europe



<P>even, but before all to Singapore; and he would take the little



<P>Nina with him.&nbsp; The child must be brought up decently.&nbsp; He

had



<P>good friends in Singapore who would take care of her and have her



<P>taught properly.&nbsp; All would be well, and that girl, upon whom the



<P>old seaman seemed to have transferred all his former affection



<P>for the mother, would be the richest woman in the East--in the



<P>world even.&nbsp; So old Lingard shouted, pacing the verandah with his



<P>heavy quarter-deck step, gesticulating with a smouldering



<P>cheroot; ragged, dishevelled, enthusiastic; and Almayer, sitting



<P>huddled up on a pile of mats, thought with dread of the



<P>separation with the only human being he loved--with greater dread



<P>still, perhaps, of the scene with his wife, the savage tigress



<P>deprived of her young.&nbsp; She will poison me, thought the poor



<P>wretch, well aware of that easy and final manner of solving&nbsp; the



<P>social, political, or family problems in Malay life.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To his great surprise she took the news very quietly, giving only



<P>him and Lingard a furtive glance, and saying not a word.&nbsp; This,



<P>however, did not prevent her the next day from jumping into the



<P>river and swimming after the boat in which Lingard was carrying



<P>away the nurse with the screaming child.&nbsp; Almayer had to give



<P>chase with his whale-boat and drag her in by the hair in the



<P>midst of cries and curses enough to make heaven fall.&nbsp; Yet after



<P>two days spent in wailing, she returned to her former mode of



<P>life, chewing betel-nut, and sitting all day amongst her women in



<P>stupefied idleness.&nbsp; She aged very rapidly after that, and only



<P>roused herself from her apathy to acknowledge by a scathing



<P>remark or an insulting exclamation the accidental presence of her



<P>husband.&nbsp; He had built for her a riverside hut in the compound



<P>where she dwelt in perfect seclusion.&nbsp; Lakamba's visits had



<P>ceased when, by a convenient decree of Providence and the help of



<P>a little scientific manipulation, the old ruler of Sambir



<P>departed this life.&nbsp; Lakamba reigned in his stead now, having



<P>been well served by his Arab friends with the Dutch authorities.



<P>Syed Abdulla was the great man and trader of the Pantai.&nbsp; Almayer



<P>lay ruined and helpless under the close-meshed net of their



<P>intrigues, owing his life only to his supposed knowledge of



<P>Lingard's valuable secret.&nbsp; Lingard had disappeared.&nbsp; He wrote



<P>once from Singapore saying the child was well, and under the care



<P>of a Mrs. Vinck, and that he himself was going to Europe to raise



<P>money for the great enterprise.&nbsp; "He was coming back soon.&nbsp;

There



<P>would be no difficulties," he wrote; "people would rush in with



<P>their money."&nbsp; Evidently they did not, for there was only one



<P>letter more from him saying he was ill, had found no relation



<P>living, but little else besides.&nbsp; Then came a complete silence.



<P>Europe had swallowed up the Rajah Laut apparently, and Almayer



<P>looked vainly westward for a ray of light out of the gloom of



<P>his shattered hopes.&nbsp; Years passed, and the rare letters from



<P>Mrs. Vinck, later on from the girl herself, were the only thing



<P>to be looked to to make life bearable amongst the triumphant



<P>savagery of the river.&nbsp; Almayer lived now alone, having even



<P>ceased to visit his debtors who would not pay, sure of Lakamba's



<P>protection.&nbsp; The faithful Sumatrese Ali cooked his rice and made



<P>his coffee, for he dared not trust any one else, and least of all



<P>his wife.&nbsp; He killed time wandering sadly in the overgrown paths



<P>round the house, visiting the ruined godowns where a few brass



<P>guns covered with verdigris and only a few broken cases of



<P>mouldering Manchester goods reminded him of the good early times



<P>when all this was full of life and merchandise, and he overlooked



<P>a busy scene on the river bank, his little daughter by his side.



<P>Now the up-country canoes glided past the little rotten wharf of



<P>Lingard and Co., to paddle up the Pantai branch, and cluster



<P>round the new jetty belonging to Abdulla.&nbsp; Not that they loved



<P>Abdulla, but they dared not trade with the man whose star had



<P>set.&nbsp; Had they done so they knew there was no mercy to be



<P>expected from Arab or Rajah; no rice to be got on credit in the



<P>times of scarcity from either; and Almayer could not help them,



<P>having at times hardly enough for himself.&nbsp; Almayer, in his



<P>isolation and despair, often envied his near neighbour the



<P>Chinaman, Jim-Eng, whom he could see stretched on a pile of cool



<P>mats, a wooden pillow under his head, an opium pipe in his



<P>nerveless fingers.&nbsp; He did not seek, however, consolation in



<P>opium--perhaps it was too expensive--perhaps his white man's



<P>pride saved him from that degradation; but most likely it was the



<P>thought of his little daughter in the far-off Straits



<P>Settlements.&nbsp; He heard from her oftener since Abdulla bought a



<P>steamer, which ran now between Singapore and the Pantai



<P>settlement every three months or so.&nbsp; Almayer felt himself nearer



<P>his daughter.&nbsp; He longed to see her, and planned a voyage to



<P>Singapore, but put off his departure from year to year, always



<P>expecting some favourable turn of fortune.&nbsp; He did not want to



<P>meet her with empty hands and with no words of hope on his lips.



<P>He could not take her back into that savage life to which he was



<P>condemned himself.&nbsp; He was also a little afraid of her.&nbsp; What



<P>would she think of him?&nbsp; He reckoned the years.&nbsp; A grown woman.



<P>A civilised woman, young and hopeful; while he felt old and



<P>hopeless, and very much like those savages round him.&nbsp; He asked



<P>himself what was going to be her future.&nbsp; He could not answer



<P>that question yet, and he dared not face her.&nbsp; And yet he longed



<P>after her.&nbsp; He hesitated for years.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His hesitation was put an end to by Nina's unexpected appearance



<P>in Sambir.&nbsp; She arrived in the steamer under the captain's care.



<P>Almayer beheld her with surprise not unmixed with wonder.&nbsp; During



<P>those ten years the child had changed into a woman, black-haired,



<P>olive-skinned, tall, and beautiful, with great sad eyes, where



<P>the startled expression common to Malay womankind was modified by



<P>a thoughtful tinge inherited from her European ancestry.&nbsp; Almayer



<P>thought with dismay of the meeting of his wife and daughter, of



<P>what this grave girl in European clothes would think of her



<P>betel-nut chewing mother, squatting in a dark hut, disorderly,



<P>half naked, and sulky.&nbsp; He also feared an outbreak of temper on



<P>the part of that pest of a woman he had hitherto managed to keep



<P>tolerably quiet, thereby saving the remnants of his dilapidated



<P>furniture.&nbsp; And he stood there before the closed door of the hut



<P>in the blazing sunshine listening to the murmur of voices,



<P>wondering what went on inside, wherefrom all the servant-maids



<P>had been expelled at the beginning of the interview, and now



<P>stood clustered by the palings with half-covered faces in a



<P>chatter of curious speculation.&nbsp; He forgot himself there trying



<P>to catch a stray word through the bamboo walls, till the captain



<P>of the steamer, who had walked up with the girl, fearing a



<P>sunstroke, took him under the arm and led him into the shade of



<P>his own verandah:&nbsp; where Nina's trunk stood already, having been



<P>landed by the steamer's men.&nbsp; As soon as Captain Ford had his



<P>glass before him and his cheroot lighted, Almayer asked for the



<P>explanation of his daughter's unexpected arrival.&nbsp; Ford said



<P>little beyond generalising in vague but violent terms upon the



<P>foolishness of women in general, and of Mrs. Vinck in particular.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You know, Kaspar," said he, in conclusion, to the excited



<P>Almayer, "it is deucedly awkward to have a half-caste girl in the



<P>house.&nbsp; There's such a lot of fools about.&nbsp; There was that

young



<P>fellow from the bank who used to ride to the Vinck bungalow early



<P>and late.&nbsp; That old woman thought it was for that Emma of hers.



<P>When she found out what he wanted exactly, there was a row, I can



<P>tell you.&nbsp; She would not have Nina--not an hour longer--in the



<P>house.&nbsp; Fact is, I heard of this affair and took the girl to my



<P>wife.&nbsp; My wife is a pretty good woman--as women go--and upon my



<P>word we would have kept the girl for you, only she would not



<P>stay.&nbsp; Now, then!&nbsp; Don't flare up, Kaspar.&nbsp; Sit still.&nbsp;

What can



<P>you do?&nbsp; It is better so.&nbsp; Let her stay with you.&nbsp; She

was never



<P>happy over there.&nbsp; Those two Vinck girls are no better than



<P>dressed-up monkeys.&nbsp; They slighted her.&nbsp; You can't make her



<P>white.&nbsp; It's no use you swearing at me.&nbsp; You can't.&nbsp;

She is a



<P>good girl for all that, but she would not tell my wife anything.



<P>If you want to know, ask her yourself; but if I was you I would



<P>leave her alone.&nbsp; You are welcome to her passage money, old



<P>fellow, if you are short now."&nbsp; And the skipper, throwing away



<P>his cigar, walked off to "wake them up on board," as he expressed



<P>it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer vainly expected to hear of the cause of his daughter's



<P>return from his daughter's lips.&nbsp; Not that day, not on any other



<P>day did she ever allude to her Singapore life.&nbsp; He did not care



<P>to ask, awed by the calm impassiveness of her face, by those



<P>solemn eyes looking past him on the great, still forests sleeping



<P>in majestic repose to the murmur of the broad river.&nbsp; He accepted



<P>the situation, happy in the gentle and protecting affection the



<P>girl showed him, fitfully enough, for she had, as she called it,



<P>her bad days when she used to visit her mother and remain long



<P>hours in the riverside hut, coming out as inscrutable as ever,



<P>but with a contemptuous look and a short word ready to answer any



<P>of his speeches.&nbsp; He got used even to that, and on those days



<P>kept quiet, although greatly alarmed by his wife's influence upon



<P>the girl.&nbsp; Otherwise Nina adapted herself wonderfully to the



<P>circumstances of a half-savage and miserable life.&nbsp; She accepted



<P>without question or apparent disgust the neglect, the decay,&nbsp; the



<P>poverty of the household, the absence of furniture, and the



<P>preponderance of rice diet on the family table.&nbsp; She lived with



<P>Almayer in the little house (now sadly decaying) built originally



<P>by Lingard for the young couple.&nbsp; The Malays eagerly discussed



<P>her arrival.&nbsp; There were at the beginning crowded levees of Malay



<P>women with their children, seeking eagerly after "Ubat" for all



<P>the ills of the flesh from the young Mem Putih.&nbsp; In the cool of



<P>the evening grave Arabs in long white shirts and yellow



<P>sleeveless jackets walked slowly on the dusty path by the



<P>riverside towards Almayer's gate, and made solemn calls upon that



<P>Unbeliever under shallow pretences of business, only to get a



<P>glimpse of the young girl in a highly decorous manner.&nbsp; Even



<P>Lakamba came out of his stockade in a great pomp of war canoes



<P>and red umbrellas, and landed on the rotten little jetty of



<P>Lingard and Co.&nbsp; He came, he said, to buy a couple of brass guns



<P>as a present to his friend the chief of Sambir Dyaks; and while



<P>Almayer, suspicious but polite, busied himself in unearthing the



<P>old popguns in the godowns, the Rajah sat on an armchair in the



<P>verandah, surrounded by his respectful retinue waiting in vain



<P>for Nina's appearance.&nbsp; She was in one of her bad days, and



<P>remained in her mother's hut watching with her the ceremonious



<P>proceedings on the verandah.&nbsp; The Rajah departed, baffled but



<P>courteous, and soon Almayer began to reap the benefit of



<P>improved relations with the ruler in the shape of the recovery of



<P>some debts, paid to him with many apologies and many a low salaam



<P>by debtors till then considered hopelessly insolvent.&nbsp; Under



<P>these improving circumstances Almayer brightened up a little.



<P>All was not lost perhaps.&nbsp; Those Arabs and Malays saw at last



<P>that he was a man of some ability, he thought.&nbsp; And he began,



<P>after his manner, to plan great things, to dream of great



<P>fortunes for himself and Nina.&nbsp; Especially for Nina!&nbsp; Under

these



<P>vivifying impulses he asked Captain Ford to write to his friends



<P>in England making inquiries after Lingard.&nbsp; Was he alive or dead?



<P>If dead, had he left any papers, documents; any indications or



<P>hints as to his great enterprise?&nbsp; Meantime he had found amongst



<P>the rubbish in one of the empty rooms a note-book belonging to



<P>the old adventurer.&nbsp; He studied the crabbed handwriting of its



<P>pages and often grew meditative over it.&nbsp; Other things also woke



<P>him up from his apathy.&nbsp; The stir made in the whole of the island



<P>by the establishment of the British Borneo Company affected even



<P>the sluggish flow of the Pantai life.&nbsp; Great changes were



<P>expected; annexation was talked of; the Arabs grew civil.



<P>Almayer began building his new house for the use of the future



<P>engineers, agents, or settlers of the new Company.&nbsp; He spent



<P>every available guilder on it with a confiding heart.&nbsp; One thing



<P>only disturbed his happiness:&nbsp; his wife came out of her



<P>seclusion, importing her green jacket, scant sarongs, shrill



<P>voice, and witch-like appearance, into his quiet life in the



<P>small bungalow.&nbsp; And his daughter seemed to accept that savage



<P>intrusion into their daily existence with wonderful equanimity.



<P>He did not like it, but dared say nothing.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter03</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER III.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>The deliberations conducted in London have a far-reaching



<P>importance, and so the decision issued from the fog-veiled



<P>offices of the Borneo Company darkened for Almayer the brilliant



<P>sunshine of the Tropics, and added another drop of bitterness to



<P>the cup of his disenchantments.&nbsp; The claim to that part of the



<P>East Coast was abandoned, leaving the Pantai river under the



<P>nominal power of Holland.&nbsp; In Sambir there was joy and



<P>excitement.&nbsp; The slaves were hurried out of sight into the forest



<P>and jungle, and the flags were run up to tall poles in the



<P>Rajah's compound in expectation of a visit from Dutch man-of-war



<P>boats.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The frigate remained anchored outside the mouth of the river, and



<P>the boats came up in tow of the steam launch, threading their way



<P>cautiously amongst a crowd of canoes filled with gaily dressed



<P>Malays.&nbsp; The officer in command listened gravely to the loyal



<P>speeches of Lakamba, returned the salaams of Abdulla, and assured



<P>those gentlemen in choice Malay of the great Rajah's--down in



<P>Batavia--friendship and goodwill towards the ruler and



<P>inhabitants of this model state of Sambir.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer from his verandah watched across the river the festive



<P>proceedings, heard the report of brass guns saluting the new flag



<P>presented to Lakamba, and the deep murmur of the crowd of



<P>spectators surging round the stockade.&nbsp; The smoke of the firing



<P>rose in white clouds on the green background of the forests, and



<P>he could not help comparing his own fleeting hopes to the rapidly



<P>disappearing vapour.&nbsp; He was by no means patriotically elated by



<P>the event, yet he had to force himself into a gracious behaviour



<P>when, the official reception being over, the naval officers of



<P>the Commission crossed the river to pay a visit to the solitary



<P>white man of whom they had heard, no doubt wishing also to catch



<P>a glimpse of his daughter.&nbsp; In that they were disappointed, Nina



<P>refusing to show herself; but they seemed easily consoled by the



<P>gin and cheroots set before them by the hospitable Almayer; and



<P>sprawling comfortably on the lame armchairs under the shade of



<P>the verandah, while the blazing sunshine outside seemed to&nbsp; set



<P>the great river simmering in the heat, they filled the little



<P>bungalow with the unusual sounds of European languages, with



<P>noise and laughter produced by naval witticisms at the expense of



<P>the fat Lakamba whom they had been complimenting so much that



<P>very morning.&nbsp; The younger men in an access of good fellowship



<P>made their host talk, and Almayer, excited by the sight of



<P>European faces, by the sound of European voices, opened his heart



<P>before the sympathising strangers, unaware of the amusement the



<P>recital of his many misfortunes caused to those future admirals.



<P>They drank his health, wished him many big diamonds and a



<P>mountain of gold, expressed even an envy of the high destinies



<P>awaiting him yet.&nbsp; Encouraged by so much friendliness, the



<P>grey-headed and foolish dreamer invited his guests to visit his



<P>new house.&nbsp; They went there through the long grass in a



<P>straggling procession while their boats were got ready for the



<P>return down the river in the cool of the evening.&nbsp; And in the



<P>great empty rooms where the tepid wind entering through the



<P>sashless windows whirled gently the dried leaves and the dust of



<P>many days of neglect, Almayer in his white jacket and flowered



<P>sarong, surrounded by a circle of glittering uniforms, stamped



<P>his foot to show the solidity of the neatly-fitting floors and



<P>expatiated upon the beauties and convenience of the building.



<P>They listened and assented, amazed by the wonderful simplicity



<P>and the foolish hopefulness of the man, till Almayer, carried



<P>away by his excitement, disclosed his regret at the non-arrival



<P>of the English, "who knew how to develop a rich country," as he



<P>expressed it.&nbsp; There was a general laugh amongst the Dutch



<P>officers at that unsophisticated statement, and a move was made



<P>towards the boats; but when Almayer, stepping cautiously on the



<P>rotten boards of the Lingard jetty, tried to approach the chief



<P>of the Commission with some timid hints anent the protection



<P>required by the Dutch subject against the wily Arabs, that salt



<P>water diplomat told him significantly that the Arabs were better



<P>subjects than Hollanders who dealt illegally in gunpowder with



<P>the Malays.&nbsp; The innocent Almayer recognised there at once the



<P>oily tongue of Abdulla and the solemn persuasiveness of Lakamba,



<P>but ere he had time to frame an indignant protest the steam



<P>launch and the string of boats moved rapidly down the river



<P>leaving him on the jetty, standing open-mouthed in his surprise



<P>and anger.&nbsp; There are thirty miles of river from Sambir to&nbsp;

the



<P>gem-like islands of the estuary where the frigate was awaiting



<P>the return of the boats.&nbsp; The moon rose long before the boats had



<P>traversed half that distance, and the black forest sleeping



<P>peacefully under her cold rays woke up that night to the ringing



<P>laughter in the small flotilla provoked by some reminiscence of



<P>Almayer's lamentable narrative.&nbsp; Salt-water jests at the poor



<P>man's expense were passed from boat to boat, the non-appearance



<P>of his daughter was commented upon with severe displeasure, and



<P>the half-finished house built for the reception of Englishmen



<P>received on that joyous night the name of "Almayer's Folly" by



<P>the unanimous vote of the lighthearted seamen.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For many weeks after this visit life in Sambir resumed its even



<P>and uneventful flow.&nbsp; Each day's sun shooting its morning rays



<P>above the tree-tops lit up the usual scene of daily activity.



<P>Nina walking on the path that formed the only street in the



<P>settlement saw the accustomed sight of men lolling on the shady



<P>side of the houses, on the high platforms; of women busily



<P>engaged in husking the daily rice; of naked brown children racing



<P>along the shady and narrow paths leading to the clearings.



<P>Jim-Eng, strolling before his house, greeted her with a friendly



<P>nod before climbing up indoors to seek his beloved opium pipe.



<P>The elder children clustered round her, daring from long



<P>acquaintance, pulling the skirts of her white robe with their



<P>dark fingers, and showing their brilliant teeth in expectation



<P>of a shower of glass beads.&nbsp; She greeted them with a quiet smile,



<P>but always had a few friendly words for a Siamese girl, a slave



<P>owned by Bulangi, whose numerous wives were said to be of a



<P>violent temper.&nbsp; Well-founded rumour said also that the domestic



<P>squabbles of that industrious cultivator ended generally in a



<P>combined assault of all his wives upon the Siamese slave.&nbsp; The



<P>girl herself never complained--perhaps from dictates of prudence,



<P>but more likely through the strange, resigned apathy of



<P>half-savage womankind.&nbsp; From early morning she was to be seen on



<P>the paths amongst the houses--by the riverside or on the jetties,



<P>the tray of pastry, it was her mission to sell, skilfully



<P>balanced on her head.&nbsp; During the great heat of the day she



<P>usually sought refuge in Almayer's campong, often finding shelter



<P>in a shady corner of the verandah, where she squatted with her



<P>tray before her, when invited by Nina.&nbsp; For "Mem Putih" she had



<P>always a smile, but the presence of Mrs. Almayer, the very sound



<P>of her shrill voice, was the signal for a hurried departure.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To this girl Nina often spoke; the other inhabitants of Sambir



<P>seldom or never heard the sound of her voice.&nbsp; They got used to



<P>the silent figure moving in their midst calm and white-robed, a



<P>being from another world and incomprehensible to them.&nbsp; Yet



<P>Nina's life for all her outward composure, for all the seeming



<P>detachment from the things and people surrounding her, was far



<P>from quiet, in consequence of Mrs. Almayer being much too active



<P>for the happiness and even safety of the household.&nbsp; She had



<P>resumed some intercourse with Lakamba, not personally, it is true



<P>(for the dignity of that potentate kept him inside his stockade),



<P>but through the agency of that potentate's prime minister,



<P>harbour master, financial adviser, and general factotum.&nbsp;&nbsp;

That



<P>gentleman--of Sulu origin--was certainly endowed with



<P>statesmanlike qualities, although he was totally devoid of



<P>personal charms.&nbsp; In truth he was perfectly repulsive, possessing



<P>only one eye and a pockmarked face, with nose and lips horribly



<P>disfigured by the small-pox.&nbsp; This unengaging individual often



<P>strolled into Almayer's garden in unofficial costume, composed of



<P>a piece of pink calico round his waist.&nbsp; There at the back of the



<P>house, squatting on his heels on scattered embers, in close



<P>proximity to the great iron boiler, where the family daily rice



<P>was being cooked by the women under Mrs. Almayer's



<P>superintendence, did that astute negotiator carry on long



<P>conversations in Sulu language with Almayer's wife.&nbsp; What the



<P>subject of their discourses was might have been guessed from the



<P>subsequent domestic scenes by Almayer's hearthstone.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Of late Almayer had taken to excursions up the river.&nbsp; In a small



<P>canoe with two paddlers and the faithful Ali for a steersman he



<P>would disappear for a few days at a time.&nbsp; All his movements were



<P>no doubt closely watched by Lakamba and Abdulla, for the man once



<P>in the confidence of Rajah Laut was supposed to be in possession



<P>of valuable secrets.&nbsp; The coast population of Borneo believes



<P>implicitly in diamonds of fabulous value, in gold mines of



<P>enormous richness in the interior.&nbsp; And all those imaginings are



<P>heightened by the difficulty of penetrating far inland,



<P>especially on the north-east coast, where the Malays and the



<P>river tribes of Dyaks or Head-hunters are eternally quarrelling.



<P>It is true enough that some gold reaches the coast in the hands



<P>of those Dyaks when, during short periods of truce in the



<P>desultory warfare, they visit the coast settlements of Malays.



<P>And so the wildest exaggerations are built up and added to on the



<P>slight basis of that fact.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer in his quality of white man--as Lingard before him--had



<P>somewhat better relations with the up-river tribes.&nbsp; Yet even his



<P>excursions were not without danger, and his returns were eagerly



<P>looked for by the impatient Lakamba.&nbsp; But every time the Rajah



<P>was disappointed.&nbsp; Vain were the conferences by the rice-pot of



<P>his factotum Babalatchi with the white man's wife.&nbsp; The white man



<P>himself was impenetrable--impenetrable to persuasion, coaxing,



<P>abuse; to soft words and shrill revilings; to desperate



<P>beseechings or murderous threats; for Mrs. Almayer, in her



<P>extreme desire to persuade her husband into an alliance with



<P>Lakamba, played upon the whole gamut of passion.&nbsp; With her soiled



<P>robe wound tightly under the armpits across her lean bosom, her



<P>scant grayish hair tumbled in disorder over her projecting



<P>cheek-bones, in suppliant attitude, she depicted with shrill



<P>volubility the advantages of close union with a man so good and



<P>so fair dealing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Why don't you go to the Rajah?" she screamed.&nbsp; "Why do you go



<P>back to those Dyaks in the great forest?&nbsp; They should be killed.



<P>You cannot kill them, you cannot; but our Rajah's men are brave!



<P>You tell the Rajah where the old white man's treasure is.&nbsp; Our



<P>Rajah is good!&nbsp; He is our very grandfather, Datu Besar!&nbsp; He

will



<P>kill those wretched Dyaks, and you shall have half the treasure.



<P>Oh, Kaspar, tell where the treasure is!&nbsp; Tell me!&nbsp; Tell me

out of



<P>the old man's surat where you read so often at night"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>On those occasions Almayer sat with rounded shoulders bending to



<P>the blast of this domestic tempest, accentuating only each pause



<P>in the torrent of his wife's eloquence by an angry growl, "There



<P>is no treasure!&nbsp; Go away, woman!"&nbsp; Exasperated by the sight

of



<P>his patiently bent back, she would at last walk round so as to



<P>face him across the table, and clasping her robe with one hand



<P>she stretched the other lean arm and claw-like hand to emphasise,



<P>in a passion of anger and contempt, the rapid rush of scathing



<P>remarks and bitter cursings heaped on the head of the man



<P>unworthy to associate with brave Malay chiefs.&nbsp; It ended



<P>generally by Almayer rising slowly, his long pipe in hand, his



<P>face set into a look of inward pain, and walking away in silence.



<P>He descended the steps and plunged into the long grass on his way



<P>to the solitude of his new house, dragging his feet in a state of



<P>physical collapse from disgust and fear before that fury.&nbsp; She



<P>followed to the head of the steps, and sent the shafts of



<P>indiscriminate abuse after the retreating form.&nbsp; And each of



<P>those scenes was concluded by a piercing shriek, reaching him far



<P>away.&nbsp; "You know, Kaspar, I am your wife! your own Christian wife



<P>after your own Blanda law!"&nbsp; For she knew that this was the



<P>bitterest thing of all; the greatest regret of that man's life.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>All these scenes Nina witnessed unmoved.&nbsp; She might have been



<P>deaf, dumb, without any feeling as far as any expression of



<P>opinion went.&nbsp; Yet oft when her father had sought the refuge of



<P>the great dusty rooms of "Almayer's Folly," and her mother,



<P>exhausted by rhetorical efforts, squatted wearily on her heels



<P>with her back against the leg of the table, Nina would approach



<P>her curiously, guarding her skirts from betel juice besprinkling



<P>the floor, and gaze down upon her as one might look into the



<P>quiescent crater of a volcano after a destructive eruption.&nbsp; Mrs.



<P>Almayer's thoughts, after these scenes, were usually turned into



<P>a channel of childhood reminiscences, and she gave them



<P>utterance in a kind of monotonous recitative--slightly



<P>disconnected, but generally describing the glories of the Sultan



<P>of Sulu, his great splendour, his power, his great prowess; the



<P>fear which benumbed the hearts of white men at the sight of his



<P>swift piratical praus.&nbsp; And these muttered statements of her



<P>grandfather's might were mixed up with bits of later



<P>recollections, where the great fight with the "White Devil's"



<P>brig and the convent life in Samarang occupied the principal



<P>place.&nbsp; At that point she usually dropped the thread of her



<P>narrative, and pulling out the little brass cross, always



<P>suspended round her neck, she contemplated it with superstitious



<P>awe.&nbsp; That superstitious feeling connected with some vague



<P>talismanic properties of the little bit of metal, and the still



<P>more hazy but terrible notion of some bad Djinns and horrible



<P>torments invented, as she thought, for her especial punishment by



<P>the good Mother Superior in case of the loss of the above charm,



<P>were Mrs. Almayer's only theological luggage for the stormy road



<P>of life.&nbsp; Mrs. Almayer had at least something tangible to cling



<P>to, but Nina, brought up under the Protestant wing of the proper



<P>Mrs. Vinck, had not even a little piece of brass to remind her of



<P>past teaching.&nbsp; And listening to the recital of those savage



<P>glories, those barbarous fights and savage feasting, to the story



<P>of deeds valorous, albeit somewhat bloodthirsty, where men of her



<P>mother's race shone far above the Orang Blanda, she felt herself



<P>irresistibly fascinated, and saw with vague surprise the narrow



<P>mantle of civilised morality, in which good-meaning people had



<P>wrapped her young soul, fall away and leave her shivering and



<P>helpless as if on the edge of some deep and unknown abyss.



<P>Strangest of all, this abyss did not frighten her when she was



<P>under the influence of the witch-like being she called her



<P>mother.&nbsp; She seemed to have forgotten in civilised surroundings



<P>her life before the time when Lingard had, so to speak, kidnapped



<P>her from Brow.&nbsp; Since then she had had Christian teaching, social



<P>education, and a good glimpse of civilised life.&nbsp; Unfortunately



<P>her teachers did not understand her nature, and the education



<P>ended in a scene of humiliation, in an outburst of contempt from



<P>white people for her mixed blood.&nbsp; She had tasted the whole



<P>bitterness of it and remembered distinctly that the virtuous Mrs.



<P>Vinck's indignation was not so much directed against the young



<P>man from the bank as against the innocent cause of that young



<P>man's infatuation.&nbsp; And there was also no doubt in her mind that



<P>the principal cause of Mrs. Vinck's indignation was the thought



<P>that such a thing should happen in a white nest, where her



<P>snow-white doves, the two Misses Vinck, had just returned from



<P>Europe, to find shelter under the maternal wing, and there await



<P>the coming of irreproachable men of their destiny.&nbsp; Not even the



<P>thought of the money so painfully scraped together by Almayer,



<P>and so punctually sent for Nina's expenses, could dissuade Mrs.



<P>Vinck from her virtuous resolve.&nbsp; Nina was sent away, and in



<P>truth the girl herself wanted to go, although a little frightened



<P>by the impending change.&nbsp; And now she had lived on the river for



<P>three years with a savage mother and a father walking about



<P>amongst pitfalls, with his head in the clouds, weak, irresolute,



<P>and unhappy.&nbsp; She had lived a life devoid of all the decencies

of



<P>civilisation, in miserable domestic conditions; she had breathed



<P>in the atmosphere of sordid plottings for gain, of the no less



<P>disgusting intrigues and crimes for lust or money; and those



<P>things, together with the domestic quarrels, were the only events



<P>of her three years' existence.&nbsp; She did not die from despair and



<P>disgust the first month, as she expected and almost hoped for.



<P>On the contrary, at the end of half a year it had seemed to her



<P>that she had known no other life.&nbsp; Her young mind having been



<P>unskilfully permitted to glance at better things, and then thrown



<P>back again into the hopeless quagmire of barbarism, full of



<P>strong and uncontrolled passions, had lost the power to



<P>discriminate.&nbsp; It seemed to Nina that there was no change and&nbsp;

no



<P>difference.&nbsp; Whether they traded in brick godowns or on the muddy



<P>river bank; whether they reached after much or little; whether



<P>they made love under the shadows of the great trees or in the



<P>shadow of the cathedral on the Singapore promenade; whether they



<P>plotted for their own ends under the protection of laws and



<P>according to the rules of Christian conduct, or whether they



<P>sought the gratification of their desires with the savage cunning



<P>and the unrestrained fierceness of natures as innocent of culture



<P>as their own immense and gloomy forests, Nina saw only the same



<P>manifestations of love and hate and of sordid greed chasing the



<P>uncertain dollar in all its multifarious and vanishing shapes.



<P>To her resolute nature, however, after all these years, the



<P>savage and uncompromising sincerity of purpose shown by her



<P>Malay kinsmen seemed at last preferable to the sleek hypocrisy,



<P>to the polite disguises, to the virtuous pretences of such white



<P>people as she had had the misfortune to come in contact with.



<P>After all it was her life; it was going to be her life, and so



<P>thinking she fell more and more under the influence of her



<P>mother.&nbsp; Seeking, in her ignorance, a better side to that life,



<P>she listened with avidity to the old woman's tales of the



<P>departed glories of the Rajahs, from whose race she had sprung,



<P>and she became gradually more indifferent, more contemptuous of



<P>the white side of her descent represented by a feeble and



<P>traditionless father.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer's difficulties were by no means diminished by the girl's



<P>presence in Sambir.&nbsp; The stir caused by her arrival had died out,



<P>it is true, and Lakamba had not renewed his visits; but about a



<P>year after the departure of the man-of-war boats the nephew of



<P>Abdulla, Syed Reshid, returned from his pilgrimage to Mecca,



<P>rejoicing in a green jacket and the proud title of Hadji.&nbsp; There



<P>was a great letting off of rockets on board the steamer which



<P>brought him in, and a great beating of drums all night in



<P>Abdulla's compound, while the feast of welcome was prolonged far



<P>into the small hours of the morning.&nbsp; Reshid was the favourite



<P>nephew and heir of Abdulla, and that loving uncle, meeting



<P>Almayer one day by the riverside, stopped politely to exchange



<P>civilities and to ask solemnly for an interview.&nbsp; Almayer



<P>suspected some attempt at a swindle, or at any rate something



<P>unpleasant, but of course consented with a great show of



<P>rejoicing.&nbsp; Accordingly the next evening, after sunset, Abdulla



<P>came, accompanied by several other grey-beards and by his nephew.



<P>That young man--of a very rakish and dissipated appearance--



<P>affected the greatest indifference as to the whole of the



<P>proceedings.&nbsp; When the torch-bearers had grouped themselves below



<P>the steps, and the visitors had seated themselves on various lame



<P>chairs, Reshid stood apart in the shadow, examining his



<P>aristocratically small hands with great attention.&nbsp; Almayer,



<P>surprised by the great solemnity of his visitors, perched himself



<P>on the corner of the table with a characteristic want of dignity



<P>quickly noted by the Arabs with grave disapproval.&nbsp; But Abdulla



<P>spoke now, looking straight past Almayer at the red curtain



<P>hanging in the doorway, where a slight tremor disclosed the



<P>presence of women on the other side.&nbsp; He began by neatly



<P>complimenting Almayer upon the long years they had dwelt together



<P>in cordial neighbourhood, and called upon Allah to give him many



<P>more years to gladden the eyes of his friends by his welcome



<P>presence.&nbsp; He made a polite allusion to the great consideration



<P>shown him (Almayer) by the Dutch "Commissie," and drew thence the



<P>flattering inference of Almayer's great importance amongst his



<P>own people.&nbsp; He--Abdulla--was also important amongst all the



<P>Arabs, and his nephew Reshid would be heir of that social



<P>position and of great riches.&nbsp; Now Reshid was a Hadji.&nbsp; He

was



<P>possessor of several Malay women, went on Abdulla, but it was



<P>time he had a favourite wife, the first of the four allowed by



<P>the Prophet.&nbsp; And, speaking with well-bred politeness, he



<P>explained further to the dumbfounded Almayer that, if he would



<P>consent to the alliance of his offspring with that true believer



<P>and virtuous man Reshid, she would be the mistress of all the



<P>splendours of Reshid's house, and first wife of the first Arab in



<P>the Islands, when he--Abdulla--was called to the joys of Paradise



<P>by Allah the All-merciful.&nbsp; "You know, Tuan," he said, in



<P>conclusion, "the other women would be her slaves, and Reshid's



<P>house is great.&nbsp; From Bombay he has brought great divans, and



<P>costly carpets, and European furniture.&nbsp; There is also a great



<P>looking-glass in a frame shining like gold.&nbsp; What could a girl



<P>want more?"&nbsp; And while Almayer looked upon him in silent dismay



<P>Abdulla spoke in a more confidential tone, waving his attendants



<P>away, and finished his speech by pointing out the material



<P>advantages of such an alliance, and offering to settle upon



<P>Almayer three thousand dollars as a sign of his sincere



<P>friendship and the price of the girl.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Poor Almayer was nearly having a fit.&nbsp; Burning with the desire

of



<P>taking Abdulla by the throat, he had but to think of his helpless



<P>position in the midst of lawless men to comprehend the necessity



<P>of diplomatic conciliation.&nbsp; He mastered his impulses, and spoke



<P>politely and coldly, saying the girl was young and as the apple



<P>of his eye.&nbsp; Tuan Reshid, a Faithful and a Hadji, would not want



<P>an infidel woman in his harem; and, seeing Abdulla smile



<P>sceptically at that last objection, he remained silent, not



<P>trusting himself to speak more, not daring to refuse point-blank,



<P>nor yet to say anything compromising.&nbsp; Abdulla understood the



<P>meaning of that silence, and rose to take leave with a grave



<P>salaam.&nbsp; He wished his friend Almayer "a thousand years," and



<P>moved down the steps, helped dutifully by Reshid.&nbsp; The torch-



<P>bearers shook their torches, scattering a shower of sparks into



<P>the river, and the cortege moved off, leaving Almayer agitated



<P>but greatly relieved by their departure.&nbsp; He dropped into a chair



<P>and watched the glimmer of the lights amongst the tree trunks



<P>till they disappeared and complete silence succeeded the tramp of



<P>feet and the murmur of voices.&nbsp; He did not move till the curtain



<P>rustled and Nina came out on the verandah and sat in the



<P>rocking-chair, where she used to spend many hours every day.&nbsp; She



<P>gave a slight rocking motion to her seat, leaning back with



<P>half-closed eyes, her long hair shading her face from the smoky



<P>light of the lamp on the table.&nbsp; Almayer looked at her furtively,



<P>but the face was as impassible as ever.&nbsp; She turned her head



<P>slightly towards her father, and, speaking, to his great



<P>surprise, in English, asked--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Was that Abdulla here?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes," said Almayer--"just gone."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And what did he want, father?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"He wanted to buy you for Reshid," answered Almayer, brutally,



<P>his anger getting the better of him, and looking at the girl as



<P>if in expectation of some outbreak of feeling.&nbsp; But Nina remained



<P>apparently unmoved, gazing dreamily into the black night outside.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Be careful, Nina," said Almayer, after a short silence and



<P>rising from his chair, "when you go paddling alone into the



<P>creeks in your canoe.&nbsp; That Reshid is a violent scoundrel, and



<P>there is no saying what he may do.&nbsp; Do you hear me?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She was standing now, ready to go in, one hand grasping the



<P>curtain in the doorway.&nbsp; She turned round, throwing her heavy



<P>tresses back by a sudden gesture.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Do you think he would dare?" she asked, quickly, and then turned



<P>again to go in, adding in a lower tone, "He would not dare.



<P>Arabs are all cowards."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almayer looked after her, astonished.&nbsp; He did not seek the repose



<P>of his hammock.&nbsp; He walked the floor absently, sometimes stopping



<P>by the balustrade to think.&nbsp; The lamp went out.&nbsp; The first

streak



<P>of dawn broke over the forest; Almayer shivered in the damp air.



<P>"I give it up," he muttered to himself, lying down wearily.



<P>"Damn those women!&nbsp; Well!&nbsp; If the girl did not look as if

she



<P>wanted to be kidnapped!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And he felt a nameless fear creep into his heart, making him



<P>shiver again.



<P>
